Preface
This master thesis is submitted as a partial fulfilment of the requirements for the degree of Master of Science
in Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The project is credited to 35 ECTS points,
written by Regitza Cammilla Egegård Hansen at the Department of Civil Engineering, Division of structural
Engineering. The thesis presents the concept of time-dependent structural reliability and decision analysis,
and is aimed for readers with underlying knowledge regarding probability theory and statistics. The report
is written in MiKTeX 2.9 and the illustrations contributed from the author is drawn in AutoCad 2019. The
project work includes the development of a generic structural reliability model implemented in a MATLAB
script. The probabilistic analysis is computed by adopting the Monte Carlo Simulation method, the computed
probabilistic results are further implemented in a pre-posterior decision analysis. The script is compatible
for MATLAB R2019a and the executing of the simulation requires exceeding computer capacity. Therefore,
HPC (High Performance Computing) provided by the DCC (DTU Computing Center) is used. The script is
configured to use MDCS (MATLAB Distributed Computing Server) on the DCC HPC LSF (Load Sharing
Facility) cluster. This allows MATLAB to be used in parallel across different nodes, which enables operating
the script under consideration and reducing the computation time.
Kgs. Lyngby Friday 1th May, 2020
I
==PAGE:8==
==PAGE:9==
Abstract
This thesis presents the concept of time-dependent structural reliability and decision analysis in the context of
bridges. It aims at analysing how to efficiently enhance the capacity of bridges using Structural Information
Sources (SIS) by considering structural reliability in an economical and predictive perspective leading to a
systematic and complete network reclassification. The work consists of a theoretical framework introducing
the concept of structural reliability and probabilistic modelling using Monto Carlo Simulation. The concept
of Bayesian pre-posterior decision analysis is outlined and integrated in the decision making framework of the
case study. The case study classify the bridge capacity by maximum possible load class. The initial bridges
design is based on a load Class 200, which was found insufficient with regard to target reliability criteria.
The theoretical framework developed in this thesis is used to asses the optimal decision given a number of
structural health information sources and classification of the bridge in terms of capacity. The associated cost
and income of the bridge system and test methods are estimated, followed by deriving a service life integrity
management and risk model. A complete cost-benefit analysis is then developed, providing illustration of
benefit models of the prior decisions, along with presentation of the net income and benefits of all relevant
strategy choices. The expected life cycle revenue, net income and expenses related to cost and risk is tabulated,
including a risk assessment discussing the influence of implementing structural information sources related
to the index of vulnerability and robustness. The optimal prior benefit is associated with a decrease in load
capacity classification compared to the initial designed capacity, the reclassification result in Class 150 linked
with the strategy of no testing. The optimal decision is quantified by maximum value of information of the test
methods, which is found to be with regard to the strategy of performing proof load test with SHM and keeping
the bridge classified as Class 200. The optimal benefit is 8461 MU, resulting in the highest relative VoI of
0.15. Test methods and obtaining additional information improve the statistics, the economic performance and
the limit states over the bridge lifetime. By implementing structural information sources, requirements can
be meet while still maintaining the bridge without load changes. Thereby, classification in terms of structural
reliability within decision analysis may be used to improve service life integrity management.

CHAPTER1
Introduction
In civil engineering, bridges are important structures due to the advantages they provide locally and nation-
ally. In particular, highway bridge structures are essential constituents in today’s infrastructure. They are
influencing factors of both economic and social development, making it possible for trucks to transport goods
as part of the import/export trade, including generating the possibility for the public population to move freely
between locations.
Bridges has been constructed and for about four thousand years. The oldest and still existing bridge in the
world is widely considered to be the Zhaozhou Bridge in the Hebei Province in China, originally constructed
approximately A.D. 600 over the Jiaohe River. Like most bridges of its day, it was based on the experience
of its master architect and stonemason, Li Chun (Zhou et al., 2017). By today’s definition, the bridge design
and construction hardly qualifies as bridge engineering practice.
As centuries have past, innovations and improvement in technology have lead to more complex designs
and bridge constructions. A major breakthrough in modern bridge design was the Iron Bridge in Shropshire,
England in 1779 utilizing cast iron and arches to cross the river Severn (Bryden et al., 1981). With the
industrial revolution in the 19th century, steel became a dominating construction material due to the high
tensile strength it provide compared to other construction materials. Since then ever larger constructions have
been undertaken for transport of both people and goods.
Today, the longest bridge is the Danyang-Kunshan Grand Bridge in China, see Figure 1.1, that opened
in June 2011. The bridge stretches approximately 164.8 km located on the rail line between Shanghai and
Nanjing. (Buckley, 2017).
Figure 1.1: Danyang-Kunshan Grand Bridge in China (Pickup, 2013, Fig. 1).
1
==PAGE:33==
1 Introduction
With the global economic growth since World War Two, the importance of highway bridges has continued
to increase. However, the combined maintenance, reimbursement and replacement costs related to the lifespan
of a bridge have also increased. Therefore, adequate assessment of the capacity has gained importance in
modern bridge engineering.
This thesis aims to analyse efficient capacity enhancement of bridges considering structural reliability in an
economical and predictive perspective. In this chapter, an understanding of reliability as a general concept and
further specified in engineering perspective is introduced. Finally, the scope of the thesis work is presented.
1.1
Definition of reliability
Reliability is a fundamental concept of this thesis, therefore the understanding of the word is of essence and
a definition of the word ”reliability” is presented. In the Oxford English dictionary, reliability is defended
as ”Ability to relied on with confidence, trustworthiness, sureness, reliableness.” (”Reliability”, n.d., para.
1.a.) and also ”The ability of a product, etc., to perform in a required manner, or produce a desired result.”
(”Reliability”, n.d., para. 1.b.). DS/EN 1990 formulates reliability as ”The ability of a structure or a structural
member to fulfil the specified requirements, including the design working life, for which it has been designed.
Reliability is usually expressed in probabilistic terms.” (CEN, 2007, para. 1.5.2.17).
The first definition is a quality that for instance a person can have, the second can be refereed to how
definite a machine, piece of equipment or a system work. The third one is more specific on a structure or
structural members, since it defines reliability in the concept of examine constructions. However, even though
all three explanations are quite different, they all have the same thing in common, whether it is from a human
or structural aspect, they define reliability as the ability to be relied upon.
1.2
Reliability engineering
The word ”reliability” was first coined by poet Samuel Taylor Coleridge in 1816 to praise his friend poet
Robert Southey (Saleh & Marais, 2006). However, many would agree that probability and statistics are the
main elements from which reliability engineering as a technical discipline has emerged. The theory of prob-
ability was established in 1654 and was confined to games of chance until 1812, when Laplace introduced
a series of new techniques, which expanded probability analysis into probability and statistics for various of
practical problems.1 The concept and idea of mass production is also essential in the development of reliability
engineering.2 The need for a new discipline capable of dealing with quality issues in large amount production
settings unfolded as an reply to the mass production. The reaction to this pressure for quality, unfolded statis-
tical quality control. A ground breaking contribution to the rise of Reliability Engineering, came from Walter
1Theory of probability was established in 1654, by Blaise Pascal and Pierre de Fermat, due to an interest of gaming and gambling.
2Mass production is the manufacture of goods in large quantities from standardized parts. The concept is mostly associated with
Henry Ford, even though he popularized it around 1910, it has been used many years before that.
2
==PAGE:34==
1.3 Outline of the thesis
Shewhart in the late 1920s and early 1930s. He was the first to apply statistical methods to quality control
problems in manufacturing, and is considered the father of statistical quality control.
During World War II, the main reason of equipment failures were due to the failures of vacuum tubes.
After the war, the US Department of Defence (DoD) had an interest to investigate the cause of these failures.
In 1952 the DoD and the American Electronics industry, established the Advisory Group on Reliability of
Electronic Equipment (AGREE)3. The technical community came to realize that a new engineering discipline
existed, after AGREE published a report the 4th of June in 1957. This date became the accepted birth of
Reliability Engineering (Coppala, 1984).
1.3
Outline of the thesis
The scope of this thesis is a systematic and complete network reclassification of bridges achieved by the iden-
tification of efficient strategies utilizing probabilistic and decision analysis. The aim is to investigate efficient
capacity enhancement of bridges using multiple information sources. The thesis will develop a demonstrator
for the identification of efficient reclassification strategies on the basis of a sequential decision analysis. An
outline of the thesis is provided here:
Chapter 1 introduces highway bridges and a definition of reliability in a engineering perspective is provided
along with the scope of the thesis.
Chapter 2 presents a literature survey of previous work regarding reliability methods, load models for high-
way bridges and deterioration models.
Chapter 3 provides a description of the relevant structural reliability theory. The chapter also covers the
probability of failure, the Monte Carlo simulation method used in the thesis and the theoretical background
for structural systems. It describes different degradation functions in order to determine the time-depended
resistance of a structure in structural reliability analysis. Furthermore, a generic degradation function is pre-
sented. It presents the classification of bridges in Denmark. The guideline for determining bridge capacity
using probability-based theory is discussed and the classification systems of bridges related to the reliability
method is reviewed. It discusses the Bayesian decision theory with utility theory, prior and posterior decision
analyses along with the pre-posterior analysis. Finally, it presents the structural information sources in the
form of structural health information and proof load testing.
Chapter 4 lists different examples of the establishment of structural system for various bridge types and the
material properties in relation to the Daniel system.
3The purpose of this group, were to recommend measures that would result in more reliable equipment, to help implement reliability
programs in government and civilian agencies, and to disseminate a better education on reliability. This development gave rise to the
first conference on reliability to be carried out in 1954.

==PAGE:35==
1 Introduction
Chapter 5 evaluates on the case study of a simple bridge structure, including the classification of the bridge
and a decision analysis of the structural information sources.
Chapter 6 concludes on the results obtained in the case study and discusses the outlook of identification of
efficient reclassification strategies.
In this chapter the scope and fundamental definitions were described. First, a general understanding of
reliability was introduced and further elaborated in an engineering perspective. Finally, the scope of the thesis
work was presented.

==PAGE:36==
CHAPTER2
Literature Survey
The concept of reliability engineering has further been used in various fields of engineering, since it origination
in 1957 . As described previous in Section 1.2 it was first used in the electronic field, but has since emerged
to other branches of engineering too. This chapter focus on structural bridge reliability and present the re-
viewed work. Applying reliability theory can require use of reliability methods. The fundamental methods
are therefore presented followed by introduction of the basic understanding of relevant probabilistic models
and probability-based assessment encompassing the state of the art literature.
2.1
Reliability methods
Several approaches has been developed over time regarding reliability methods. Surrogate Model-based meth-
ods, Matrix-based System Reliability method (MSR), First-Order Reliability Method (FORM), Second-Order
Reliability Method (SORM) and Monte Carlo Simulation (MCS)-based methods. (Su et al., 2017; Kang et
al., 2008 Faber, 2012; Melchers, 1999).
The individual method has its own advantages and disadvantages. The decision process regarding the most
suitable method for the present problem, is based upon the system structure and the conditions. Surrogate
model-based methods are beneficial when an outcome is not easily and/or directly measured, or in situations
with simulations that can take up to several days. In these cases, the methods allows for surrogate models,
also known as approximate models, to be used instead (Koziel et al., 2011). If the component probabilities
has incomplete information and/or the statistical dependence in a system, it will be suitable and sufficient to
apply the Matrix-based System Reliability method (MSR) (Kang et al., 2008). 1 The advantages of FORM
and SORM are mainly that they are simple and apparent physical interpretations and do not usually require
excessively large computation time. The drawback of these methods is that they do not give the exact results
for the probabilities in question for most practical structures (Ditlevsen & Madsen, 1996, Ch. 6). In situations
where no analytic solution exists and no general numerical procedure is available, Monte Carlo Simulation
method is favourable. Slow convergence and high computational expense, particularly for small probability
problems are downsides of the method (Skowronek, 2010).
The accuracy of reliability prediction is also effected by the uncertainty quantification. Some uncertainties
are related to the lack of knowledge of the studied phenomenon or system behaviour, and therefore have the
possibility to be reduced if some conceivable efforts are undertaken. These are referred to as epistemic uncer-
1Compared to other methods, the MSR method can estimate probability and handle the system event in a more convenient way, since
it is possible to describe any general system event in a simply matrix form.
5
==PAGE:37==
2 Literature Survey
tainties and covers uncertainties associated with modelling errors or imprecision. 2 Uncertainties associated
with measuring observations and those due to the statistical modelling of parameters, both covering the choice
of probabilistic models and evaluation of their parameters, are also epistemic uncertainties (Koduru, 2015).
The aleatory uncertainties can be described as those that can not be reduced by presenting additional data
or enhance the model process. Therefore, they should be considered as inherent or intrinsic with regard to
analysed phenomenon. Examples of aleatory or random uncertainties can be fluctuating loads due to the
environment of operation, wind velocity, humidity and to some extent material properties are often defined as
aleatory uncertainties, since there are no means to control them (Lelièvre et al., 2016).
2.2
Load models
The are two fundamental load models, dead load and variable load. The dead loads are considered to act
permanently, they are stationary and unable to be reformed. Self-weight is considered a dead load, which
in DS/EN 1900 is recommended to be modelled as normal distributed (CEN, 2003). The permanent loads
consist of the weight of the structure and the soil pressure on the structure. In reliability, modelling distinguish
between actual self-weight and the weight of coatings, railings etc. (Scholten et al., 2004).
The variable loads covers wind, seismic and live load. The live load is the applied acting forces on the
bridges, of the loads to be modelled on a highway bridge, the most variable are those referred to as traffic
load. The variability of the traffic loads does not only come from the stochastic variables describing the
individual vehicles themselves, i.e. weight of the vehicles, axle spacing, speed, impact, etc. but also from the
probability of multiple presence, both longitudinally within individual lane, or transversely in multiple lanes
(O’Connor & Enevoldsen, 2009). In order to model the traffic load knowledge regarding number of trucks,
type, frequency and speed is needed. In Denmark, the road directory has records of the daily frequency of
vehicles, which is required to obtain the live load data (O’Connor & Enevoldsen, 2009). The live load models
for bridges, are established differently in the individually codes and guidelines. DS/EN 1991-2 operates
with deterministic analysis to determine the load model (CEN, 2003 instead of other approaches that handles
probabilistic distribution.
2.3
Deterioration models
Deterioration is the process of becoming progressively worse. The type of deterioration and its rate, depends
on the material used for construction of the bridge and the environmental attacks. Various materials in conjunc-
tion with their exposures leads to different deterioration process models. Examples of distress mechanisms
for concrete structures includes Alkali-Silica Reactivity (ASR), sulphate attacks, freeze-thaw cycling and re-
2Modelling errors and impression emerge from the choice of particular mathematical models to describe the real behaviour of phys-
ical phenomena, knowledge regarding the boundary and operational conditions of such models, the adopted scheme for resolution and
numerical approximations.
6
==PAGE:38==
2.4 Probability-based assessment of bridges
inforcement corrosion3 (Barbosa et al., 2016; González et al., 1995). The probabilistic modeling of concrete
chloride corrosion in the marine environment has also been investigated (Qin & Faber, 2012; Schneider et
al., 2014). The modelling of corrosion, chloride and carbon dioxide corrosion (Visser et al., 2000). Steel
structures are also subjected to fatigue in corrosion degradation (Straub, 2004). There are many studies that
have focused on modeling the structural deterioration of bridges. These models are investigated by laboratory
tests, statistical analysis and mathematical modeling (Ghosh & Padgett, 2015; Rendell et al., 2002; Papadakis
et al., 1996; Enright & Frangopol, 1998).
2.4
Probability-based assessment of bridges
Reliability is usually expressed in probabilistic terms, in a probability-based classification is a bridge reliability
analysis conducted by safety index method in order to determine the load-bearing capacity. The result is what
can be called a bridge classification and the modelling involves bridge specific load, resistance4 and associated
uncertainty models. The classic classification method in DS/EN 1990 exclusive acknowledge the reliability
of the bridge, but the reliability does not directly announce any information regarding the quantity of either
resistance or load. Therefore, a classification system, managed by the Danish Road Directorate (DRD) has
been developed, in such a way that information about the capacity of the bridge, is expressed out from the
classification of the bridge. The classification system is based on the heavy vehicles, such that the bridge is
classified as the maximum load class it can sustain while achieving its acceptance criteria. The principle is that
both the bridges and the heavy vehicle should be classified in comparable classes (O’Connor & Enevoldsen,
2009). The advantages is that ones the bridge is classified, the police and administrators of the bridge, can
decide if a specific heavy vehicle can pass the bridge (Scholten et al., 2004).
This thesis work encompasses modelling and classifying a simple bridge structure. For that purpose the
classification system proposed by the DRD, in order to classify the bridge capacity and load models in com-
parable classes, is incorporated.
2.5
Chapter conclusion on literature survey
In this chapter, five different quantitative reliability methods were presented, out from investigating their
different advantageous and disadvantageous it can be concluded that the Monte Carlo Simulation technique is
the most preferable for the current case study. For the loads models, both a permanent dead load representing
the bridge self-weight and a variable live load representing the traffic load should be established in the further
works. There is a various of different deterioration models, however, since the aim of the study is to establish
3Corrosion is a common deterioration that occurs in bridge structures. Corrosion is the deterioration of a metal as a result of chemical
reactions between it and the surrounding environment. Corrosion can occur when the steel structure is exposed to water, soil or open
atmosphere. Corrosion can also occur in reinforced concrete, besides the fact that the steel is loosing strength, the corrosion can cause
cracking, spalling and delamination of the concrete cover ( Bonnerup , 2009)
4The resistance of a system is the capacity of the system or component to withstands actions without material failure e.g. bending,
buckling, tension etc. (CEN, 2007).

a generic system a generic deterioration model can be chosen. The probability-based assessment with regard
to the classification of the structural system, presented by DRD, will be used in the further analysis to achieve
capacity expressed in terms of maximum sustained load class.

CHAPTER3
Theoretical Background
on Bridge Reliability and
Assessment
According to a report from 2015 published by the Danish Road Directorate (DDR), Denmark has 7042 bridges,
with a span of minimum 2 m. (DRD, 2015). Every year the municipalities and the Danish Ministry of Trans-
port disburse millions of DKK on reinforcement and in some cases replacement of the bridges, so they can
care the still increasing heavier loads from especially trucks, that moves on the Danish road network. Part of
that investment might be unnecessary, since studies shows that the actually capacity of the bridges is much
higher, than originally classified according to the calculation methods used today.
The reliability of a structure is its ability to fulfil its design purpose for a specified reference period. During
the service life, different kind strategies can be taken. The optimal decision can be established incoparting the
concept of decision analysis. The chapter aims to present the theortical background in bridge reliabiliity and
assessment, that outlines the framwork for the thesis case study. Section 3.1 introduces the basic for establishin
time-dependt, structural system using Monte Carlo Simulation. Section 3.2 present the classfication process
and the target reliability with regard to consequnces. Section 3.3.3 present the Bayesian decision analysis
encompassing probability updating and pre-posterioer decision analysis by estlishing life-cycle benefits and
establish the value of information. Section 3.4.1 presents common characterizations of the distinctive risks,
consequences and system indicators, along with the mathematical approach concerning risk analysis. Finally,
structural information sources are presented and discussed in Section 3.5. The chapter ends with a conclusion.
3.1
Structural reliability theory
The reliability of a structure is its ability to fulfil its design purpose for a specified reference period. This
section aims at presenting the theoretical process of establishing the structural reliability. Section 3.1.1 in-
troduces the functional relation between random variables for a simple case and the approach of establishing
the probability failure is expressed based on the functional relation. Section 3.1.2 presents the Monte Carlo
simulation technique, along with computational generation of the random variables, in order to establish the
reliabilities. The concepts of structural system illustration and probability establishment is described consider-
9
==PAGE:41==
3 Theoretical Background on Bridge Reliability and Assessment
ing both non-redundant, redundant and combined systems, followed by clarification of dealing with correlated
properties. Finally, Section 3.1.5 covers the concept of time dependent reliability related to structural system
performance investigation over time.
3.1.1
Probabilistic structural analysis
The reliability of a structure or structural element is presented for a simple case having two independent
random variables, R and S, where R is the resistance variable and S the load effect variable (Thoft-Christensen
& Murotsu, 1986, Ch. 1). Reliability depends on the failure probability and failure is often deemed to occur
when an applied load effect is greater than the structural resistance. Having the failure criterion R −S ≤0,
then the functional relation can be expressed by a limit state function
g(x) = R −S ≤0,
(3.1)
where g(x) is the limit state function, the components of the vector x are realizations of the basic random
variables X. A failure event can be described by the limit state function g(x) following way
F = {g(x) ≤0}.
(3.2)
The failure event F is simply defined as a set of realizations of the function g(x), which is zero or negative.
3.1.1.1
Probability of failure
Structural reliability is a measure of the safety level of a structure and is concerned with the calculation of
the probability of a defined failure. Probabilistic structural analysis requires establishment of the limit state
function and an identification of the variables involved in that function, a description of the statistical param-
eters of each variable (example mean and variance) and the calculation of the probability of violation of that
limit state function. For the basic structural problem outlined in Equation (3.1), having defined the failure
event in Equation (3.2) the probability of failure Pf may be determined by the integral shown in Equation
(3.3) (Melchers, 1999, Ch. 3)
Pf =
∫
g(x)≤0
fX(x)dx,
(3.3)
where fX(x) is the joint probability density function of the random variables X. However, the integral is
non-trivial to solve and numerical approximations are expedient.
3.1.2
Structural reliability method
Simulations techniques have in general demonstrated their value, particularly for problems where the repre-
sentation of the limit state function is not differentiable or when several design points contribute to the failure
10
==PAGE:42==
3.1 Structural reliability theory
probability.
The integral in Equation (3.3) is non-trivial to solve thus numerical integration can be used. The integral
may be estimated by so-called simulations techniques. As stated in Section 2.1, various simulations techniques
exists. However, all simulations techniques has their origin in the so-called crude Monte Carlo Simulation
(Ditlevsen & Madsen, 1996, Ch. 9). The reliability method used in this thesis work, is the Monte Carlo
Simulation and the principles of this simulation technique are outlined in the following.
3.1.2.1
Monte Carlo Simulation technique solving non-trivial integrals
For structural reliability problems a transformation of the integration boundaries is accomplished by intro-
ducing the indicator function I. The basis for simulation techniques is therefore illustrate by rewriting the
probability integral in Equation (3.3), by implementing an indicator function (Faber, 2012, Ch. 6)
Pf =
∫
I[g(x) ≤0]fX (x) dx,
(3.4)
where the integration domain is changed from being part of the sample space of the vector
X = (X1, X2, ....X3)T for which g(x) ≤0, to the entire sample space of X. This means in the simplest
approach that each random variable Xj, randomly to give a sample value xj, is checked to see whether or
not the value satisfy the limit state state function g(x) ≤0 (Melchers, 1999, Ch. 3). The integration domain
can be changed, due to the fact that the indicator function I [g(x) ≤0] takes on a value of 1 if the term in the
brackets is true, or 0 if the term in the brackets is false.
The MCS method is the most direct method of calculating the probability of failure. Samples of the
random variables are generated and the limit state function evaluated for each set. The probability of failure is
calculated as a ratio of the number of fails to the number of trials. Equation (3.4) is seen to yield the expected
value of the indicator function (Faber, 2012, Ch. 6). Having N realizations of the vector X, i.e xj where
j = 1, 2....N are sampled, then the probability of failure can be established following the sample statistics
that
Pf = 1
N
N
∑
j=1
I[g(x) ≤0]
(3.5)
is an unbiased estimator failure Pf. N is the total number of samples, I[] is the indicator function and g(x)
is the limit state function. The accuracy of the MCS method increases with the number of trials (Melchers,
1999, Ch. 3). However, this becomes impractical when the indicator function in Equation (3.5) requires
a computationally expensive numerical analysis such as a finite element calculation. Thus, this approach is
inefficient when dealing with low probabilities of failure because a very large sample set is required. However,
it has been implemented in numerous structural problems (Ditlevsen & Madsen, 1996, Ch. 9).
The principle of the crude Monte Carlo simulations technique depends directly on the use of Equation
(3.4). A large number of realizations of the basic random variables X i.e xj, j = 1, 2....N are generated (or
simulated) and for each of the outcomes xj, it is examined whether or not the limit state function taken in xj
11
==PAGE:43==
3 Theoretical Background on Bridge Reliability and Assessment
is positive. All the simulations, for which the result is not positive counted, the number is requested (nf) and
after N simulations the failure probability Pf may be estimated through
Pf = nf
N ,
(3.6)
which may be seen as a sample expected value of the probability of failure. In fact, for N →∞the estimate
of the failure probability becomes exact (Faber, 2012, Ch. 6). However, simulations are often costly in
computation time and the uncertainty of the estimate is therefore of interest. The coefficient of variation of the
estimate is corresponding to 1/√nf. This way it is possible to determine the minimum number of simulations,
which would still provide an acceptable result. In order to obtain a coefficient of variation at 10%, then the
number of fails should be 100, i.e. nf = 100. Using Monte Carlo simulation to pursue the probability of
failure to 10−4, then the number of simulations N is 106. If Pf should be 10−5, then N = 107 and so on.
3.1.2.2
Random numbers and variables attained in simulation process
The Monte Carlo Simulation is based on computational algorithms that depend on repeated random sampling
to achieve numerical results. The underlying concept is to use randomness to solve problems that might be
deterministic in principle (Ditlevsen & Madsen, 1996, Ch. 9). Therefore is the generation of random numbers
of importance. A random number generator (RNG) is the generation of a sequence of numbers, that can not be
reasonably predicted better than by randomness. Random numbers was in the early days obtained by manual
techniques.1. Technical developments later made it possible to connect physical devices, to computers in
order to obtain the random numbers.2. However, when it comes to computer-simulations, either mechanical
or physical devices are often used today3. This also mean that most of today’s RNGs are not based on physical
devices, but on simple algorithms, which can easily be implemented on a computer (Rubinstein & Kroese,
2016, Gentle et al., 2004). They are fast, requires little storage space and can readily reproduce a given
sequence of random numbers. Importantly, a good RNG captures all the important statistical properties of
true random sequence, even though this sequence is generated by a deterministic algorithm with a specific
distribution. For this reason, these generations are sometimes called pseudo-random and the generated is
called pseudo-random number generators (PRNG). The numbers are random in the sense that, on average,
they pass statistical tests regarding their distribution and correlation. The differ from true random numbers in
that they are generated by an algorithm, rather than a truly random process. Most computer programs already
contain a built-in PRNG. (Melchers, 1999, Ch. 3).
Random number generators have the ability of running the same sequence of random numbers again by
starting from the same random seed, which enables reproducibility. The user is typically requested only
to input the initial seed, X0, and upon simulation the RNG produces a sequence of independent, uniform
(0, 1) random variables. Transformation is applied to these uniform random variates, to generate random
1Manual techniques such as coin flipping, dice rolling, card shuffling and roulette spinning
2Physical devises such as noise diodes and Geiger counters
3This is due to the fact that the methods are too slow for general use and the generated sequences can not be reproduced, further have
the methods also a lack of reliability
12

3.1.3
Structural system representation
Most structures have a number of possible failure modes, which must be taken into account upon the reliability
estimation of structural systems (Thoft-Christensen & Murotsu, 1986, Ch. 1). The structural system is formu-
lated using the hypothesis that the structure can be decomposed into n identifiable components. Certain types
of systems frequently arise in practice and serve to illustrate the idea of the structure function. There are two
fundamental types of logical systems; the series system and the parallel system. If it is not possible to solve
a structural problem by using one of the simple system presented, it may be possible to solve the problem by
viewing it as a combination of simple substructures. The substructures can be either series or parallel systems,
which means that the structure can be presented as a combined system.
The following presents a short review of the theory related to the two systems, series and parallel, and a
combined system.
3.1.3.1
Series system
A series system is configured such that if any one of the system’s components fails, the entire system fails.
Conceptually, a series system is one that is as weak as its weakest link (Thoft-Christensen & Murotsu, 1986,
Ch. 3). A graphical representation of a series system is shown in Figure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: Illustration of a series system with a number of n components.
In the series system, the components are connected in a string, see Figure 3.2, which means that failure
of any element causes the overall failure. The combination of failure modes in a series system are statically
determinate (non-redundant). The probability of failure of a series system with n components is defined as
(JCSS, 2001, Sec. 10.4)
Pf = P
( n

irrespective of the random nature of the material strength (Melchers, 1999, Ch. 5). Hence

3.1.3.2
Parallel system
A parallel system is configured such that the system can survive even if a number of components fails. As long
as not all of the components fails, it will not necessarily lead to failure of the total system (Thoft-Christensen
& Murotsu, 1986, Ch. 2).4 A graphical representation of a parallel system of n components is shown in Figure
3.3.
Figure 3.3: Illustration of a parallel system with a number of n components.
In a parallel system, as presented in Figure 3.3, the overall failure occurs only after all the elements have
failed. The reason for this is that failure of one element will result in the load effect now being distributed

Since a redistribution of the load effects takes place in a redundant structural system after failure of one or
more of the structural components, it becomes important in parallel systems to describe the behaviour of the
structural elements. This material properties can be accounted for by establishment of the Daniels system is
required. A Daniels system can be understood as logical parallel system considering the mechanical behaviour
(Daniels, 1945), see Figure 3.4.

The behaviour of the components in a Daniels system can be distinguished as either ideal brittle or ideal
ductile. If a components load-bearing capacity is completely exhausted after failure, it is said to be perfectly
brittle. If the element after failure has a load-bearing capacity equal to the load at failure, the element is said
to be perfectly ductile (Thoft-Christensen & Murotsu, 1986, Ch. 5). In Figure 3.4, the behaviour of perfectly
brittle and perfectly ductile element are shown. The question in a brittle system is whether or not the remaining
elements are able to prevent failure. The probability of failure for a ideal brittle system is
Pf,B = P

& Thöns, 2016). When a member of a brittle material fails in tension, the residual strength is equal to zero,
while in case of a ductile material the yield strength of the member is taken as the residual strength. The
probability of failure for a ideal ductile Daniels system is
Pf,D = P

The behaviour of a parallel structural system will therefore to a high degree depend on whether the failure
elements are ductile of brittle. Far from all types of structural components material behaviour can be described
as either perfectly brittle or perfectly ductile. Every kind of combination in between exist, i.e. some, but not
all, of the failure strength capacity is retained.
3.1.3.3
Combined system
Most structural systems encompass combinations of series and parallel configurations. In those situations,
establishment of a system’s structural reliability can be managed through separation of the system into homo-
geneous subsystems (Mojtaba, 2018). The reliability is then obtained separately for each of these subsystems,
17
==PAGE:49==
3 Theoretical Background on Bridge Reliability and Assessment
considering them individually as a unit. The reliability for each subsystem, is then considered in the overall
system. An illustration for a combined system is given in Figure 3.5, the system consists of 7 components,
divided into two subsystems, A and B. Subsystem A consists of two identical components in parallel system
and subsystem B is composed of five identical components and the two subsystems are in a series system.
Figure 3.5: A combined system with 7 components, consist of two parallel subsystems, A and B, in a series
system. Subsystem A consist of 2 components and subsystem B of 5 components.
The dependency of the components can be different in the two subsystems, and further can dependency be-
tween the two subsystems also exist. If components in the same subsystem have different dependency, ex-
ample if component 3 and 4 are independent while 5, 6 and 7 are dependent with some degree of correlation,
then the system should be broken down to three subsystems. The components dependency should therefore
also be taken into consideration in order to obtain correct establishment of the structural system.
3.1.4
Coefficient of correlation to account for dependency
In the following, an approach related to accounting for correlation of structural elements is presented. The
simple and classical concept in the structural reliability theory assumes that components/subsystems are statis-
tically independent.5 However, in many real structural systems the assumptions are not justified, e.g., suspen-
sion or cable-stayed bridges, where failure of any cables will increase the risk of failure (Tharmabala & Nowak,
1987). The correlation coefficient measures the extent to which two variables tend to change together, which
can be either linear or non-linear. Two examples of non-linear correlations are the Kendall and the Spearman
5Statistically independent means that the failure of one component/subsystem will not effect the function/resistance of the surviving
components/subsystems.

methods. 6 The methods evaluates the monotonic relationship between two continuous or ordinal variables.
In a monotonic relationship, the variables tend to change together, but not necessarily at a constant rate. The
Pearson correlation evaluates the linear relationship between two continuous variables.7
The following theory consider the simplest form of correlation, namely linear correlation (Faber, 2012, Ch.
4). When the variables X are correlated, it is necessary to generate numbers from a multivariate distribution.
This is contained by establishing the covariance matrix (Thoft-Christensen & Murotsu, 1986, Ch.2). The

3.1.5
Time dependent reliability
The basic terminology and theory for a simple case of time-independent structural reliability is outlined in
Sections 3.1.1 and 3.1.1.1. For structural systems, the time dependent reliability is usually more desired,
since the matter of interest often is the likelihood of structural failure in each of the years after construction
(Kuschel & Rackwitz, 2000). If a structure is not subject to any maintenance, the probability of failure will
increase during the service life. This is due to the fact that both the loads and material properties changes with
time. The basic variables X will be considered as functions of time and the limit state function will then also
be considered a function of time with
g [x (t)] = R(t) −S(t) ≤0.
(3.24)
The load effect S(t) can either be an continuous time varying load, with an arbitrary small increment in time,
increasing the value S(t) at each time. The load effect can also be considered as re-applied precisely at the
very instant time t, assuming structural survival at a time previous to t. The probability of failure Pf(t) may
be determined by rewriting Equation (3.3), such that the integral depends on time t
Pf =
∫
g[x(t)]≤0
fX(t) [x(t)] dx (t) .
(3.25)
The instantaneous failure probability can be integrated over time equal zero to time t by means of Equation
(3.25) to obtain the failure probability over the period. The classical approach is to consider the integration
transferred to the load effect, which is assumed to be representable over the total time period, by an extreme
value distribution. The resistance it assumed essentially time invariant at that time period (Kuschel & Rack-
witz, 2000; Melchers, 1999, Ch. 6).8 Usually, however, data are not availing for a sufficiently long period of
time, and records periods generally must be used to synthesize the extreme value distribution.
A refinement is to consider the total time in shorter periods of time, such as a month or a year and apply
extreme value theory within that period. The resistance is then time invariant within the given reference period,
the approach enables the possibility for change in the resistance at each reference period. The resistance of a
structure deteriorates due to distress mechanism and ageing. The deterioration-induced resistance reduction
can be modelled as the reduction of the initial component resistance R0 in dependency of a time-dependent
damage evolution (Qin & Faber, 2012; Thöns & Schneider, 2016). Having a damage evolution D (t), the
resistance is continuously with
R (t) = R0 (1 −D (t)) .
(3.26)
The function 1−D (t) is also in some literature referred to as the resistance degradation function. The degrada-
tion function is denoted W (t). In Section 2.3 various examples of deterioration mechanisms are mentioned.
8The probability distribution for S may be obtained directly by fitting an appropriate probability distribution function to observed
extreme value data of past observations and assuming it will be applicable also for the future.

3.1 Structural reliability theory
This section will take a look at the degradation function associated to different deterioration mechanisms
(Enright & Frangopol, 1998; Naus et al., 1996).
The following presents five different mechanismen, investigating corrosion, sulphate attack, diffusion
controlled mechanism, traffic spray damage and fatigue. To model the corrosion mechanism, Equation (3.27)
represent the degradation function
W(t) = 1 −αL × t,
(3.27)

(3.28) can be used to determine the degradation function, where αP is a coefficient that may vary due to the
degradation rate
W(t) = 1 −αP × t2,
(3.28)
where αP is a coefficient that may vary due to the degradation rate. Equation (3.29) is used to model diffusion
controlled degradation mechanism
W(t) = 1 −αs
√

W(t) = 1 −k1t + k2t,
(3.30)
where k1 and k2 are random variables. In the case of fatigue damage, the Palmgren-Miner rule can be used.
Having a load spectrum with variable amplitude, the fatigue damage is assumed to accumulate linearly with
the number of load cycles (Bonnerup , 2009). A specimen subjected to a stress S1 has a life span of N1 cycle,
of for S2 its life span is N2 cycles. The damaged accumulated at stress S1 after n1 cycles is n1/N1. For n2
cycles at S2 the damage will be n2/N2. For a range of fatigue loading the partial damages sum-up to the total
damage, so

The five mentioned deterioration models all presents examples of the degradation functions used for es-
tablishment of deterioration-induced resistance reduction in dependency of the time t.
3.1.5.1
Generic degradation function
Structures are subjected to effects of deterioration models. The reliability of the structural system is time-
dependent and may decrease with time, thus causing the risk of structural failure to increase. Despite the
variety of the mechanisms, a general and generic formulation may be found. The damage evolution may
21
==PAGE:53==
3 Theoretical Background on Bridge Reliability and Assessment
be represented by a random process D (t) modelling the material deterioration during the service life. The
deterioration could be regarded as an accumulation process with time
D (t) =
t
∑
i=1
∆D,i,
(3.32)
with the annual increments ∆D,i having the same distribution with uncertain expected value MµD and constant
standard deviation σ∆D (Qin et al., 2015). The model is based on the Palmgren-Miner rule of linear damage
accumulation over load cycles.
3.2
Classification of bridges
The general rules regarding classification of bridges provides a good and necessary baseline for the classifi-
cation of bridge capacity. Thus the general rules forms an effective basis for calculation of the capacity of
bridges, it is relevant to notice, that the rules are general and hereby conservative, in order to comprehend all
types of bridges. Typical the rules are conservative regarding requirements to the load magnitude, placement
of the load, load event, dynamic increment, capacity calculations and model requirements. In this section,
classification approaches and target requirements related to the consequences class and relative cost of safety
measure for different service limit states is reviewed.
3.2.1
Classification using reliability method
Conservative design rules can result in marginal expensive new bridges although, when it comes to existing
bridges, the conservative rules can mean the difference between repairs or bridge replacement. It is therefore
expedient, after identification of the problems by analyses using the general rules, to investigate the bridge
performances using more advanced bridge specific calculations methods if possible, before considering invest-
ing in a reinforcement or replacement project. Examples of calculations methods could be 3D finite element,
plastic, dynamic or probability-based analyses. The purpose of using other methods is to remove some of the
excess safety, that the conservative general rules introduces. The purpose is to gain savings by averting un-
necessary reinforcement or replacement projects. Applying reliability based approach when assessing bridge
capacity, enables satisfaction of safety requirements, even though the exact same bridge might not comply
with all safety requirements when applying the general analysis (CEN, 2007; O’Connor & Enevoldsen, 2009,
Scholten et al., 2004).
3.2.1.1
The Danish Road Directorate guideline for bridge classification
Bridges can be classified in many different ways, either according to the type, material of construction, length
of the bridge, function, etcetera. The classification of relevance for this study is the classification according
to the capacity of the bridge. Different classification approaches has been obtained during the years. The
22
==PAGE:54==
3.2 Classification of bridges
following described is a Danish guideline for probability-based assessment of highway bridges, published in
2004 by the Danish Road Directorate. The classification system is based upon the idea that the bridges and
the vehicle should be classified in comparable classes. Therefore, the classification of bridges is performed
using scale specific reference vehicles designated standard vehicles. It represents vehicles with a total weight
ranging from 10-500 t, representing 22 different classes. The guideline itself consists of seven chapters, in-
cluding the process in the assessment of the classification, as shown in Figure 3.6 Scholten et al., 2004).9 It
should be noted that the guideline does not provide information for modelling time dependent deterioration
of structures.
The process of assessing the bridge classification requires establishment of the specific bridge and its
undertaken conditions. The chapters in the guideline follows the steps described in the flowchart in Figure
3.6. Since classification is an iterative process, the first iteration is executed based on a chosen load class. It
is therefore suggested that the first iteration is carried out with a load class there with reasonable adequate can
be consider genuine for the bridge under consideration, in order to avoid unnecessary iterations.
The first step in the classification process is to define the classification case that applies to the structure
under consideration. The classification cases are:
• Normal passage is defined as passage involving vehicles with both no restrictions or position. Vehicles
may be placed at the most severe location in respect of the load effect under consideration with speed
of passage inducing the maximum dynamic amplification.
• Conditional passage type I is defined as passage involving vehicles with no restrictions on speed, but
position is restricted to lanes within the defined carriageway.
• Conditional passage type II is defined as passage involving vehicles with both restricted speed (10km/hr)
and position. Vehicles may be placed, within lanes, at the most severe location in respect of the load
effect under consideration with speed of passage reduced to limit dynamic amplification.
• Conditional passage type III is defined as passage involving only the heaviest vehicle with restricted
speed 10km/hr and position. The vehicle is placed in the least severe location in respect of the load effect
under consideration with speed of passage reduced to limit dynamic amplification. This case simulates
temporary bridge closure and prescribed passage route for the heavy transport.
9Chapter 1 is an introduction, which outlines the purpose of the guideline, regarding road bridges in the public road network. Chapter
2 describes the probability-based approach for classification of road bridges. The chapter includes a diagram, which describes the process
in the assessment of the classification, as shown in Figure 3.6. Chapter 3 defines the DRD’s requirements regarding structural safety in
both the ultimate and serviceability limit state. In Chapter 4, specifications on methods for the probability-based safety analysis are given
with guidelines apprehension model uncertainties incorporation in the analyses. Chapter 5 concerns load distribution models, including
requirements and specifications concerning performance of uncertainty modelling. This chapter describes traffic load distribution apply-
ing Poisson models. Furthermore, models on special heavy transports and ordinary trucks are given, along with information concerning
modelling transverse and longitudinal load location in lanes and specifications for modelling of dynamic impact factors. In chapter 6,
information for modelling material strengths is provided. For the three generations of the deterministic codes of practice in Denmark
from 1949, 1973 and 1984 (DRD, 2015), it is specified how concrete compression strength, yield stress of various reinforcement types
and yield stress of structural steel must be modelled as stochastic variables. In addition, the chapter includes information regarding model
uncertainties in modelling material strength. Chapter 7 describes the implementing of new information from tests in the probability-based
assessment. Standard techniques for parameter estimating and reliability updating are described.

==PAGE:55==

Figure 3.6: Flowchart of probability-based classification according to the Danish Road Directorate (DRD)
guideline (O’Connor & Enevoldsen, 2009, Fig. 2).

==PAGE:56==
3.2 Classification of bridges
The above description provides a small insight regarding the extent of information required to be known, en-
compassing merely the usage of the bridge. The next step is to determine critical limit state violation from
deterministic analysis, the critical limit state is determined on basis of the deterministic analysis. It is cost-
efficient first to identify specific problems using the general approach, because it narrows down the following
probabilistic modelling of the problems with related limit states requiring more careful consideration. Descrip-
tion regarding the modelling of stochastic variables, defining the ordinary traffic and the standard vehicle for
the required classification are already explained somewhat in Section 2 and Section 3.2.1.1. Step 3 concerns
the definition of the required safety index, which is of great importance when applying structural reliability
theory and therefore is further presented in Section 3.2.1.2. After defining safety index βt and reliability β,
these are compared. If the determined reliability is lower or higher than the safety index, the class should be
reduced or increased, respectively, and the traffic load recalculated. This process is repeated until the point
where the reliability is equal to the safety index. If it is not possible to obtain a reliability equal to the safety
index, the value closest to, but still higher than, the safety index, is accepted. In conclusion, if the safety index
is reached with the vehicle load of Class 150, then the classification of the bridge is Class 150.
3.2.1.2
Consequences related to safety
In terms of a reliability based approach the structural risk acceptance criteria correspond to a required minimum
reliability, which is referred to as the safety index βt in the Figure 3.6, but will hereinafter be defined as the
target reliability βt. The reliability is defined as
βt = −Φ−1 (Pf) ,
(3.33)
where Φ−1 is the inverse standardized Gaussian distribution function, of the annual probability of failure Pf.
The requirements to the safety of the structure are consequently expressed in terms of the accepted minimum
reliability index or the accepted maximum failure probability, see Equation (3.33). In a practical approach the
required reliability of the structure is controlled by:
• A set of assumptions about quality assurance and quality management measures; these measures are for
example related to design and construction supervision and are intended to avoid gross errors.
• Formal failure probability requirements, conditional upon these assumptions, defined by specified target
values for the various classes of structures and structural members.
Consequences Classes (CC) may be established for the purpose of reliability differentiation, by considering
failure or malfunction of a structure as given in Table 3.1.

The consequences classes may be associated with the three Reliability Classes (RC), RC1, RC2 and RC3. In
DS/EN 1990 the target reliability for a given reference period is provided by reliability class. According to the
Joint Committee on Structural Safety (JCSS), a classification into consequences classes is based on the ratio
ρ, defined as the ratio between total costs (i.e. construction costs plus direct failure costs) and construction
costs (JCSS, 2001, Sec. 7). The three consequence classes with their associated ratio is:
• Consequence Class 1 - minor consequences of failure - ρ is less than approximately 2.
• Consequence Class 2 - moderate consequences of failure - ρ is between 2 and 5.
• Consequence Class 3 - Large consequences of failure - ρ is between 5 and 10.

26
==PAGE:58==
3.3 Bayesian decision theory
• Type I: ductile failure with remaining capacity due to strengthening.
• Type II: ductile failure without remaining capacity.
• Type III: brittle failure.
If a structural element is demonstrating ductile behaviour, then some kind of warning, i.e. cracks, is appearing
before collapse is anticipated.10 These warnings enables measures to be taken to avoid severe consequences,
which permits a lower reliability, compared to structural elements collapsing suddenly without warning. The
values provided in Table 3.2 relates to the structural system or in approximation to the dominant failure mode
or structural component dominating system failure at ultimate limit state. A structure with numerous failure
modes being equally critical, should be designed with a high reliability. The serviceability limit states are
reached before collapse, i.e. prior to the ultimate limit states. These states are the limits where the structure
becomes unfit for its intended service. It is important to distinguish between the irreversible and reversible
limit states.11. For the reversible serviceability limit state, no general values are given by JCSS, however, the
irreversible serviceability limit states tentative target values are given in Table 3.3. A variation from the target
serviceability index of the order of 0.3 can be considered.

3.3
Bayesian decision theory
For an engineer, the ultimate task is to establish consistent decision basis for the planning, design, manufac-
turing, construction, operation and management of engineering industry. Decisions should be based on the
maximized overall life cycles benefits, which fulfil the requirements given regarding safety of personnel and
environment specified by legislation or society.
The Bayesian decision analysis, as presented by Raiffa and Schlaifer (1961), is an optimization that builds
upon the classical decision theory developed by Von Neumann and Morgenstern (1947), which provides the
fundamental basis for the Bayesian decision analysis. The Bayesian decision analysis, takes into account
10Ductile behaviour shows remaining load-bearing capacity after failure
11Irreversible states is where no consequences of actions exceeding the specified service requirements will remain when the actions
are removed
27
==PAGE:59==
3 Theoretical Background on Bridge Reliability and Assessment
the expert judgement, current science, respected theories and evidence that might be summarized within a
probabilistic expert system. The decision maker needs to apply such relevant domain knowledge on the given
problem. Thereby, the decision maker contributes with information relevant for a given problem and uses the
probabilistic information to gain the optimal decision.
This section presents the utility theory in Section 3.3.1, followed by a discussion of the maximization
of the utility and updating probability with regard to new information in Section 3.3.2, then further explains
the decision analysis with regard to buying additional information with yet unknown information outcome,
outlining different approaches for the decision analysis in Section 3.3.3. Finally, the concept of Value of
Information is presented.
3.3.1
Fundamental utility theory
The utility theory is the foundation of both the Bayesian and classical decision theory. The Bayesian decision
theory is an essential theory applied in this thesis work, the concept of the utility theory will be presented
briefly. Having a set of possible events X = (X1, X2....Xn), where n is the number of outcomes, an index is
assigned to the events in such manner, that a higher index number, implicates that the current event is preferred
over event assigned with lower index value. The decision maker will be presented with several actions a,
where each action leads to the probability of the different events occurring. The probability assigned for the
events, therefore differs in relation to the action chosen; i.e. action a1 will lead to event X1 with probability
P [X1]a1 and to event X2 with probability P [X2]a1 etcetera. Equivalent, action a2 will lead to event X1 with
probability P [X1]a2 and so on for all other alternatives. The probabilities of the events for each action, must
therefore fulfil
P [X1]ai + P [X2]ai + ....P [Xn]ai = 1,
(3.34)
where i is the number of the current action ai, from a set of potential acts a. The numerical index utility u, can
be assigned on the events, in which action is taken. The index u is appointed in such manner, that the most
preferably decision, is the one with the largest expected utility (Straub, 2004).
3.3.2
Probability updating
The previous section introduces the fundamental regarding utility theory. The Bayesian decision theory re-
quires both the prior and updated probabilities. The prior decision analysis is the decision with given/known
information. At this state all the relevant information on the true state X is available. The probabilistic de-
scription P [Xl] of the state of nature X is called a prior description and called P ′ [Xl]. Describing the state of
nature by a prior probability function, f ′
X (Xl), the analysis that aims to identify the action a, which maximizes
the expected utility E [u], is
maxau(a) = maxaEX [u (a, Xl)] = maxa
∫
X
u (a, Xl) f ′
X (Xl) dXl.
(3.35)
28
==PAGE:60==
3.3 Bayesian decision theory
The utility u in Equation (3.35), is a function of a and Xl. The posterior decision analysis principle is identical
to the prior analysis, except that new information, for example obtained by inspections, is available and is taken
into account (Straub, 2004, Ch. 2). The updated probability also called posterior probability may be evaluated
using Bayes’ rule. The probability of the state Xi, given additional information zk, can be obtained as
P ′′ [Xi|zk] =
P [zk|Xi] P ′ [Xi]
∑
i P [zk|Xi] P ′ [Xi],
(3.36)
where P [zk|Xi] is the likelihood, which is the probability of obtaining the observation zk given the true state
of nature Xi. ∑
i P [zk|Xi] P ′ [Xi] is the probability of obtaining the information zk (Faber, 2012, Ch. 2).
Equation (3.36) can also be expressed as
P ′′ [Xi|zk] = P [Xi ∩zk]
P [zk]
.
(3.37)
,
where the joint probability P [Xi ∩zk] is equal P [zk|Xi] × P ′ [Xi].
3.3.3
Pre-posterior decision analysis
The theory regarding fundamental utility and probability updating is implemented in the following in order
to present a pre-posterior decision analysis. The analysis deals with yet ”unknown” information, so when
experiments are planned but the results is still unknown, it is based on assumptions and knowledge of belief
(Faber, 2012, Ch. 7). In other words, the applied decision theory is concerned with the logic analysis of choice
among courses of action when
• The consequences of any course of action will depend upon the ”state of the world”.
• The true state is yet unknown.
• It is possible at a cost to obtain additional information about the true state.
Assumptions has been taken concerning the person responsible for the decision making. First of all it has been
assumed that the person has limited the problem to choices within a well-defined set of contenders, by elimi-
nating possible courses of action defined as being unworthy of further consideration. Furthermore, it has been
assumed that the person has enough knowledge to choose wisely among these contenders. The choices should
be logically consistent with the basic preferences concerning consequences and basic judgement concerning
the true state of the world (Raiffa & Schlaifer, 1961). In order to make the decision analysis, the following
informations need to be known:
• Space of terminal acts a = {ak}. Possible actions that can be taken prior and after additional information.
The decision maker wishes to select a single act ak from domain a of potential acts.
29
==PAGE:61==
3 Theoretical Background on Bridge Reliability and Assessment
• State space Xk = {Xl}. The consequences of adopting terminal act a depends on some ”true state of
nature” which can not be predicted with certainty. Each potential state will be labelled by a Xl with
domain Xk.
• Family of information i = {ii}. The possible informations which should be attached to each Xl in Xk,
the decision maker may select a single information ii from a family of i of potential information.
• Sample space Zi = {Zj}. Each potential outcome of a potential information ii will be labelled by a Zj
with domain Zi.
The basic data could represent decision problems such as, reassessment of existing structures or decommis-
sioning of facilities that have become obsolete. Since the number of alternative actions can be extremely large,
a framework representing the systematic analysis of the corresponding consequences, can be of expediency.
An example of a systematic analysis tool could be a decision tree, which conveniently represent the decision
framework. Figure 3.7 shows a simple decision tree.
Figure 3.7: Illustration of decision tree for the assessment of the value of information containing decision
nodes (rectangles) and chance nodes (circles) (Thöns,2018b, Fig. 1).
In the decision tree the branches are associated with decision nodes (rectangles) and chance nodes (circles).
The decision nodes are denoting the decision variables and the chance nodes are describing a probabilistic
model associated with the chance nodes. The first two basic branches represent whether or not to consider
additional information, which is related to a decision. The decisions are based on the quantification and
maximization of the benefit or utility for each of the choices accounting for their uncertain outcomes. The
decision problem illustrated in Figure 3.7, has the outcome of different decisions given by benefit b. In a
30
==PAGE:62==
3.3 Bayesian decision theory
situation where the benefits of the different activities are not implicated, the optimization could be performed
on the expected costs, i.e. the maximization of the utility, is the minimum expected cost (Thöns, 2018b).

problem. There are two basic modes of analysis that can be used to find the decision which will maximize
the expected utility. The analysis of the decision model can be performed in either extensive or normal form,
however, in both cases the prior analysis (the branch with no additional information) is analysed the same

3.3.4
Value of information analysis
Value of information (VoI) analysis encompass valuing the expected gain from reducing uncertainty through
some form of data collection. The origins of VoI lies in the work of Raiffa and Schlaifer on statistical decision
theory, back from 1961 (Raiffa & Schlaifer, 1961). The framework takes basis in the classical decision analy-
sis, which consists of an objective function to be maximized and a choice between courses of action leading to
utilities with respect to the objective function. It is possible to invest in research to reduce uncertainty in the
utilities, but such information can be costly and will thus have a negative impact on the utility. The question
is therefore whether it is worth investing in additional information to reduce uncertainty before revisiting the
decision. For this evaluation, VoI analyses are performed (Wilson, 2014).
The following notation is equivalent to the one used in the previous section, the fundamental decision of
considering additional and yet unknown information or not can then be based upon maximization of the VoI
(Thöns, 2018b), yielding
V (i∗

value of information analysis is based on the Expected Value of Sample Information (EVSI), in other words,
EVSI is a pre-posterior VoI analysis. The VoI analysis can also be performed considering the Conditional
Value of Sample Information (CVSI). The conditional value is associated with the outcome of the information
Zj. The utility for the VoI related to an outcome Zj is expressed as

The two value of information presented are based on sample information, the concept of perfect informa-
tion is also worth mentioning. Perfect information removes all uncertainty about the outcomes for the decision
alternatives. While there rarely is an option in the real world where a decision would actually remove all uncer-
tainty, the value of perfect information provides an easily calculated benchmark about the worth of collection
additional information (Wilson, 2014). If all the available options for collecting information costs more than
the value of perfect information, then these options does not need to be analysed in further detail. This is
because imperfect information can not be worth more than perfect information, no source of information can
be worth more than the perfect information.
3.4
Risk Assessment
Risk assessment involves the identification of hazards events and their accompanied consequences of poten-
tial structure malfunction or failure. Risk is a notion rather common in the everyday conversations. It is
frequently used to communicate that people are uncertain about the condition of the activity, item or issue
under consideration. However, in the context of engineering decisions, risk has a different meaning. This
section elaborates on the definition of risk assessment in engineering. Firstly, Section 3.4.1 presents common
characterizations of the distinctive risks, consequences and system indicators, along with the mathematical ap-
proach concerning risk analysis. Secondly, Section 3.4.2 comprises an analytical description of risk analysis
regarding generic system characterization of a roadway network.
3.4.1
Analysis and quantification of system risk
In the context of engineering decision making, risk is typically defined as the expected consequences associ-
ated with a given activity. Risk analysis is defined in general terms within the concept of decision theory. In
33
==PAGE:65==
3 Theoretical Background on Bridge Reliability and Assessment
general, the risk assessment in engineering decision analyses can be expressed by considering the generic sys-
tem representation illustrated in Figure 3.8. A system should consist of a spatial and temporal representation of
all constituents, required to describe the interrelations between all relevant exposures and their consequences.
Figure 3.8: Generic system representation in risk assessments (JCSS, 2008, Fig. 4).
The exposure events, such as loads and attacks (i.e. by chemical substances), are acting on the constituents
of the system, causing either damage or failure of the constituents (JCSS, 2008). Based on the combination
of exposure events leading to constituent failure events and corresponding consequences, follow-up conse-
quences may occur. The consequences can be categorized in to either direct or indirect consequences. The
direct consequences are thus defined as causing structural malfunction, the consequences are associated with
either damage or failure of the constituents in the system and will no result in system failure. The direct
consequences can lead to follow-up consequences, resulting in loss of structural functionality. Indirect conse-
quences may then be defined as any consequence associated with the loss of the functionalities of the system
and by any specific characteristic of the joint state of the constituents and the direct consequences themselves
(JCSS, 2008). The indirect consequences is defined as any consequence beyond direct consequence.
The presented framework facilitates a Bayesian approach to risk assessment and full utilization of risk
indicators. The risk indicators contains information about the risk and can be presumed as any characteristic
of the system or its constituents that can be observed or measured.
The first task in the risk analysis of a system is defining the potential exposure events. These exposures
have probabilistic characterization p (EXk), EX represent exposure and k = 1..nEXP, where nEXP indicate
the number of exposures. Each of the individual constituent, has a discrete set of component damage states
Cij, where i = 1, 2..nCON, with nCON being the number of constituent and j = 1, 2..nCi, where nCi is the
total number of different damage states of constituent i. Instead of Cij, Cl can be used. Hereby, the direct
consequences cD (Cl) is associated with the lth of nCSTA possible different states of damage of all constituents
34
==PAGE:66==
3.4 Risk Assessment
Cl (JCSS, 2008). p (Cl | EXk) describes the probability of all constituents conditional on the exposures events.
The direct risk RD is expressed as the expected value of p (Cl | EXk) cD (Cl) due to direct consequences of
all possible exposure events nEXP and all constituent damage states nCST A. The direct risk is expressed as
RD =
nEXP
∑

∑

(3.45)
The vulnerability can then be quantified through the index IV , which is defined as the ratio between the direct
risks and the total amount of the asset or combination of assets recognizing relevant exposures and a specified
time frame
IV = RD

(3.46)
where VA represents the characteristic utilized to measure the value of the direct risk.12
The indirect consequences cID (Sm, cD (Cl)) are associated with the available different states nSSTA of the
constituent Sm due to exposure EXk. The probability of indirect consequences conditional on a given state
of the constituents Cl, the associated direct consequences cD (Cl) and the exposures EXk, is described by
p (Sm | Cl, EXk). The corresponding conditional risk is p (Sm | Cl, EXk) cID (Sm, cD (Cl)). The risk due
to indirect consequences is assessed through the expected value of the indirect consequences in regard to all
