which allows for decisions to be assessed with regard to their impact on robustness. The risk assessment
of a load bearing structure is considered, the risk indicators can then be any observable quantity which can
be associated with the loading of the structure (exposure), the strength of the components of the structure
(vulnerability) and the redundancy, ductility, effectiveness of condition control and maintenance (robustness).
3.4.2
System characterizations at different scales
The various costs related to a system can be included in either the categories of direct or indirect consequences,
depending on the purpose of the analysis and the scale at which the analysis is performed. For instance, if the
analysis is performed on an individual bridge, the monetary value of the human health expenses and life loss
12The monetary value of the constituents of the system, the extent of lost human lives or the evaluated conditions of the environment,
can be examples of value of the direct risk.
35
==PAGE:67==
3 Theoretical Background on Bridge Reliability and Assessment
due to collapse can be included in the direct consequences since the costs are induced by system failure of the
bridge and not by a single component. The direct consequences would be failure or damage of the individual
components. Figure 3.9 shows the risk assessment conditional on the scale for three systems.
Figure 3.9: Generic system characterizations for a roadway network and infrastructure object at different
scales in terms of exposure, vulnerability and robustness (JCSS, 2008, Fig. 5).
Figure 3.9 presents an example on how the risk assessment would look, depending on the scale. If the
purpose of the risk analysis is to evaluate a highway network, the entire bridge is acting as a component in the
system. The cost of the activities to restore the full functionality or the reconstruction of the bridge is among
the direct consequences. In addition, the failure of a bridge may cause severe accidents involving the vehicles
on the bridge at the instant of failure. Therefore, the cost of accidents, the human health and life costs are
included in the direct consequences. Also, the costs associated with the environmental damage and with the
impact on the general public, are considered among direct consequences.
The indirect costs of the bridge failure impact the users of the highway route where the bridge is located
and are often called “user costs”. Following the failure of a bridge, the users of the highway need to by-pass
the bridge with alternative routes. However, the capacity of the alternative routes may be less than that of
the original route, causing traffic congestion, and the alternative routes may be longer, which might effect the
user preferences on deciding their travel paths. Therefore, computation of the indirect consequences of bridge
failure within a network requires a complex traffic assessment analysis accounting for the detour length of the
bridge.
36
==PAGE:68==
3.5 The concept of structural information sources
3.5
The concept of structural information sources
All bridges experience deterioration over time due to environmental conditions and traffic load. The asso-
ciated analytical forecasting are subject to high uncertainties, due to the lack of specific knowledge on the
development of the state of the bridge and the conditions surrounding this complex infrastructure. Structural
information sources, such as sensors or load testing, can be used to gather information that can be imple-
mented by updating the probabilities and improve the knowledge with regard to uncertainties in the structural
reliability assessment, during the service life. In this section, the historical development and concept of two
structural information sources is presented. Section 3.5.1 introduces structural health monitoring and Section
3.5.2 introduces proof load testing.
3.5.1
Introducing structural health honitoring
Given the significant role that infrastructure present in providing society with a high quality of living standards,
verifying design assumptions, tracking structural performance over a structure’s life, and rapidly identifying
unsafe structural conditions emerged from damage and deterioration, is relevant information for the reduction
of risks. The existence of uncertainties in the assessment of the system performance is an influencing factor
with regard to risks throughout the service life of engineered structures (Wang et al., 2014).
Structural health monitoring is a non-destructive in-situ structural sensing and evaluation method, the
structures are monitored using a variety of sensors attached to, or embedded in, the structure. The monitor and
analysis of structural characteristics, including structural response, is adopted for the intention of estimating
the severity of damage/deterioration conditions and evaluate the consequences on the structure thereof in
terms of response, capacity, and service life (Karbhari, 2005). Structural health monitoring aims to provide
a diagnosis of the ”state” of the constituent materials, of the various components, and of the full assembly of
these components representing the structure as whole, giving at every moment during the life of a structure
(Balageas, 2014).
There are examples of permanent structural monitoring on operational civil engineering structures that
dates back to the 1940s. An early example on bridges is the monitoring system installed on the Golden Gate
Bridge between 1945 and 1946, which consisted of ten Hall accelerometers13, which monitored vertical deck
accelerations under wind excitation (Wang et al., 2014). Figure 3.10 shows a modern accelerometer, today
accelerometers are widely used either for short or long term control of the dynamic behaviour of a structure
(Shahsavari, 2020).
13An accelerometer is an electromechanical device used for measuring acceleration forces through single or multi-axis directions.
37
==PAGE:69==
3 Theoretical Background on Bridge Reliability and Assessment
Figure 3.10: Accelerometers installed on a structural system for the purpose of structural health monitoring
(Shahsavari, 2020, Fig. 2).
There are many different types of sensor systems used for structural health monitoring on bridges, as an
example, the Stonecutters Bridge in Hong Kong, which was completed in 2009, and has over 1700 sensors
installed. Figure 3.11 presents the layout of the sensory systems on the Stonecutters Bridge (Dascotte, 2011).14
Figure 3.11: Layout of sensory systems on Stonecutters Bridge (Dascotte, 2011, Fig. 1).
The area of SHM evolved in the second half of the last century, where engineers became challenged by
more complex construction projects and measurement technologies were emerged. Particularly the expansion
14Figure 3.11 lists a number of sensors that will not be explained in detail, but they include both structural and environmental sensors
to measure for example displacement, strain, acceleration, temperature and wind conditions (Dascotte, 2011).
38
==PAGE:70==
3.5 The concept of structural information sources
of the impact that monitoring systems could have with regard to the behaviour of structural systems over
their complete life-cycles in relation to asses their long-term performance and health (i.e., structural health
monitoring), was given attention.15 The interest of structural health monitoring has grown not only within the
structural engineering research community, but also in the broader areas of civil and mechanical engineering.
Today, SHM is widely applied to various types of infrastructure systems including bridges, roads, pipelines,
dams, and power grids, all vital resources that provide basic services to society (Wang et al., 2014; Thöns,
2018b).
3.5.2
Introducing proof load test
Proof load testing is a load testing method that provides information about the resistance of a structure or
element. By performing proof load test on a structure or element, it can be concluded that the resistance
of the tested object is at least equal to the presented test load, if the object survives the loading undamaged
(Brüske & Thöns, 2017). Proof load test is not the only form of load test that can be applied as alternative
or complement to the assessment of bridges through analytical calculations. During the years a considerable
amount of bridge data test have been accumulated by various jurisdictions. Load tests are executed in various
types of structures in numerous countries worldwide. To begin with, the key objective of load tests was to
confirm the design assumptions and the anticipated behaviour throughout the design phase as well as the
quality of the construction. 16 In the early 20th century the first high level of testing accompanied by great
development of analytical techniques, where perceived, which lead to the design codes, used widely today,
where the design process of structures is mostly calculation based (Hall & Tsai, 1989). Examples of different
types of load test used in the present include:
• A behaviour test is performed to confirm the results of the analysis methods and can be served as com-
plement to the assessment process. The applied test load is lower than the ultimate load, if also kept
below the design loads, thus theoretically, it is safe and does not carry any risk of damage or collapse.
The low loading levels results in information only regarding the load distribution. The behaviour test
does not provide much information on the resistance.
• A stress history test can be conducted when the stress distribution in fatigue-prone areas of the bridge,
wants to be established. The fatigue life of the bridge is verified, using data continuously obtained due
to ordinary passing of vehicles on the bridge.
• A diagnostic test is performed to establish the source of issue when the analytical approach is especially
difficult to perform.
15In the beginning of the 1970s a desire to do more with sensors and the sensing system derived from the structural engineers. The
development within the area of SHM has accelerated the last decades, due to the use of electronic data processing and storage technologies
and the field further expanded to data analysis algorithms.
16Later on, they have furthermore been used as an effective method to update the analysis models and to evaluate the capacity of
existing structures (Nowak & Tharmabala, 1988). Load testing in generally have had another level of importance in the past, since the
development of mathematical models for design by calculations, were combined with the practice of load testing.
39
==PAGE:71==
3 Theoretical Background on Bridge Reliability and Assessment
• The ultimate load tests, also known as up-to-failure tests, are carried out in situations where the ultimate
load carrying capacity is wished to be determined. The tests are performed by loading the bridge to
failure, so it is mostly done for elderly bridges, and are normally conducted to confirm the assessment
by some analytical methods. These kinds of tests, was mostly performed when it came to establishing
the analytical methods. After validating the analytical methods, those can be used to predict ultimate
strength of other similar structures.
Proof load tests are commonly applied to existing structures, during their service life. Proof load tests are
typically executed under circumstances where there is reason to doubt that the reliability of a structure satisfies
the acceptance criteria. There might be reason to believe, or evidence, that the original design assumptions
are no longer adequate or for the purpose of regularly checking the structure in order to verify the validity of
original design assumptions (Nishijima & Faber, 2007).
During the execution of proof load test, the structure is subjected to high static load, in order to gain
knowledge about structural behaviour. The load that the bridge can withstand without suffering failure during
the load test is described as the test load. During the performance of the load test it is necessary to take several
precautions such as good planning, gradual implementation of the test load and control the responses of the
structure in order to not exceed the elastic limit of the material (Nowak & Tharmabala, 1988).
Since bridges are subjected to very high loads during the proof load tests, there is always the possibility
that the bridge can be permanently damaged. However, this scenario is extremely unlikely. Indeed, more
than 250 bridges were subjected to load test assays in Ontario, Canada, without showing any signs of damage
(Bakht & Jaeger 1990). A successful proof load test proves instantly that the resistance of the bridge is greater
than the proof load. As a result, this reduces the uncertainty related to the resistance and thereby increases its
reliability (Fujino & Lind, 1977). Furthermore, a proof load test may provide additional useful information
about bridge properties and performance. As such, the results of the successful proof load test may be utilized
to confirm a satisfied reliability of the tested structure under given circumstances (Moses et al., 1994; Nowak
& Tharmabala, 1988). A proof load test is a relevant and beneficial test method in the context of assessment
and reassessment of existing highway network and bridge structures (Stewart & Val, 1999). The main reason
for this is the fact that they are subjected to ageing along with changing and generally increasing traffic loads
which makes the use of structural information sources necessary. The results obtained from proof load testing
enable decision making concerning further maintenance actions such as e.g. inspection, repair or replacement
(Nishijima & Faber, 2007).
3.6
Chapter conclusion on theoretical background on bridge
reliability and assessment
The chapter introduced the theory of structural reliability.
40
==PAGE:72==
3.6 Chapter conclusion on theoretical background on bridge reliability and assessment
In Section 3.1, a functional relation between random variables for a simple case and an approach of es-
tablishing the probability failure was expressed. In cases where numerical approximations are expedient, the
Monte Carlo method can be used along with the ability of computational generation of random variables.
Structural systems of both non-redundant, redundant and combined systems were presented, along with the
Daniels system, which takes into account the material properties and therefore are relevant for structural
reliability analyses. Finally, the relevance of investigating structural reliability over a service lifetime was
discussed, hence a time-dependent reliability approach was introduced.
Section 3.2 presented structural classification related to target reliabilities and provided an insight into a
few of the many conditions that needs to be established in order to execute reliability methods. Therefore,
generic models and simplifications are necessaries to insure the limitation and relevance of this study. Funda-
mentally, there are two basic usage limitations; with the first being the structure reaching the state where it is
no longer acknowledged as serviceable in order to insure human safety, and secondly the ultimate limit state
where the structure has reached the limit of collapse probability. This stresses the importance of accounting
for the different limit state functions in structural analyses.
Section 3.3 presented the usage of probabilistic information based on Bayesian decision analysis. The
optimal decision process depends on the decision makers ability to apply relevant domain knowledge on the
given problem. In addition, the mathematical utility approach for the optimal decision analysis should also
be considered. The pre-posterior decision analysis is the main approach chosen for this thesis in order to
gain knowledge on buying additional information during the service life. Based on the outlined different
methodologies, the normal form enables the possibility of executing the decision analysis process without
establishing the posterior analysis. This provides the ability of excluding the computation of the probability
of the additional information outcome, which also have the benefit of limiting the required usage of computer
capacity.
Section 3.4 elaborated on the definition of risk assessment within civil engineering. The risk assessment
was based on dividing the cause of the damage of the structure into direct or indirect consequences. Depending
on the system level, analysis of the direct and indirect consequences differs. The direct consequences were
found to be associated with damage or failure of the constituents, while indirect were associated with structural
failure. Based on the distinguished generic system characterization of a road network and infrastructure object,
it was found that computation of the indirect and direct consequences of bridge failure requires a complex
traffic assignment analysis accounting for the detour length of the bridge.
Finally, Section 3.5 presented and discussed structural information sources as a solution to lower the uncer-
tainties with regards to the state of the bridge and the conditions of the surrounding environment. SHM was
found to be ideal for monitoring the structural responses and characteristics in order to estimate the severity
of the bridge. On the other hand, proof load testing was found to be preferable in scenarios where information
regarding the resistance capacity was limited.

CHAPTER4
Approach for Bridge’s
Structural System Design
Engineering the structural reliability approach on bridge structures requires an establishment of the structural
system. In this chapter, it is described how the structural system can be converted into a structure function in
order to quantify and evaluate the reliability of a bridge system. Section 4.1 covers system representations.
Initially, an introduction of the mathematical theory of system reliability is provided, embracing the concept
of path and cut vectors by means of failure state. Further is an illustration of the simple structural system
with inherent redundancy in reliability network presented. The mathematical approach is applied in order to
establish the reliability of the system and outline the advantage of presenting a structural system in a graph
representation. Section 4.2 presents different bridge structures along with illustrations of the structural system
and explanations of the process. For the primarily bridge, i.e. a truss bridge in Section 4.2.1, a more detailed
explanation and illustration regarding the establishment of the structural system is provided, due to the purpose
of providing a more elaborated insight regarding the process. The chapter ends with a conclusion.
4.1
Authentication of structure function
A real structure consists in general of many elements and for each element several failure modes may be rel-
evant. In this section a short introduction to the mathematical theory of system reliability is given (Cremona,
2013). The construction of a structural system enables the assessment of the structure function for a structural
system.
A structure function ϕ is expressed in the following way:
• At any instance it may be in only one of two states: either functioning or failed
• The structure can be decomposed into components (or failure modes), each component is either func-
tioning or failed.
The theory makes it possible to decide the failure of complex systems, since it allows assembling of the
components in sets. First step is to express the state of the structure in terms of its elements. The state indicator
variables Fi is

conversion is implemented to assure that the indication of failed or functioning elements and/or structures
is equivalent with the theory presented in Section 3.1.3, such that the structure function is a established as a
failure function.
A structure’s path and cut sets can be defined in the process of establishing the structure function. A path
vector L is defined as a set of elements of the system S which, if they all function, assures that the system
functions even if the components not belonging to L has failed.

Consider a simple structure of a two span composite two-girder bridge, i.e. a continuous slab-on-girder
bridge with two beams. It is assumed that the two main girders has intermediate cross beams, with additional
redundancy1 provided by the transverse rigidity of the concrete deck, acts like continuous diaphragms. The
illustration of the bridge example is seen in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Illustration of two-span composite two-girder bridge (Cremona, 2013, Fig. 3.17).
The critical failures are located between the supports and at mid-span. Let components E represent the
cross beams in a parallel system, which provides additional redundancy to the system and the two girders
denoted A and B. Figure 4.2 characterize the structure by its components of failure modes in a diagram.
The illustration in Figure 4.2 is a diagram of the components established from the design of the structure.
However, treating the illustrations as a block diagram in regard to the function of the structural system will be
incorrect. The reason for this is that in case of a component failure, the redundancy will not be accounted for in
1A structure can have both active and passive redundancy. Active redundancy exists when all components perform at the same time,
even if they are not strictly necessary, they can still not be excluded. Example four wheels in a truck axle or a pre-stressing cable with
many strands. For passive redundancy the components or substructure are in reserve, meaning that they are not expected to be used under
normal circumstances, for example a spare tire in a car.

correct manner. Further explanations considering this obstacle is presented in the latter, with a correct display-

let Figure 4.2 represent the structural system in regard to establishing the structure function assuming both
girders intact, meaning that A1, A2, A3, B1, B2 and B3 are functioning. The load is considered uniformly
distributed, the structural participation of the diaphragm is therefore neglected. The minimal path sets are
then:
R1 = {A1, A2, A3},
R2 = {B1, B2, B3}.
If the probability of failure for all the components in both girder A and B is presumed to be 0.1, then the

ρ1 = ρ2 = 1 −(1 −0.1)3 = 0.271.
Then the structure function and failure probability becomes
ϕ (F) = 0.271 × 0.271 = 0.07344
The reliability of the system with one or more components in a failed state can be evaluated using the previous
presented method. Assuming that B1 has failed, the diaphragms then becomes active in order to ensure load
distribution. The minimal path sets are:
R1 = {A1, A2, A3}
R2 = {A1, A2, E3, B3}
R3 = {A1, E2, B2, B3}
R4 = {A1, E2, B2, E3, A3}.
E1 and E4 are not essential in the whole system working, since they are not part of a minimal path vector.

==PAGE:78==
4.1 Authentication of structure function
The reason path vector {A1, E2, B2, B3, E4} is not minimal is because element E4 can fail without failure
of the structure. This is incorrect in regard to the load distribution. If component B1 is failing, component E1
and E2 becomes active and very essential. If both B1 and E1 fails there will be an area of the bridge which
is not supported. Therefore, Figure 4.2 is a incorrect block diagram for the system. The physical influence
from the cross-beams in regard to load distribution needs to be accounted for in correctly manner. The block
diagram for the bridge system presented in Figure 4.1 and 4.2 can be illustrated as seen in Figure 4.3.
Figure 4.3: Structural system presented as a block diagram for a two-span composite two-girder bridge.
Figure 4.3 insure that the correct cross-beams becomes active and essential, in case of failure of a girder
component. If a girder component fails, the system survival now depends on the two neighbouring cross-
beams and the opposite girder component. It is seen that the block diagram becomes quite comprehensive,
since each component needs to be represented at least twice. The minimal path sets in case of failure of B1
then becomes
R1 = {A1, A2, A3},
R2 = {E1, A1, E2, E2, B2, B3}.
Having that the probability of failure has increased to 0.2 in the remaining girder components due to the failure
of B1 and that probability of failure for the diaphragms are 0.7. The minimal path structure ρj for the two
minimal path sets Rj are
ρ1 = 1 −(1 −A1)(1 −A2)(1 −A3) = 1 −(1 −0.2)3 = 0.49,
ρ2 = 1 −(1 −E1)(1 −A1)(1 −E2)(1 −B2)(1 −B3) = 1 −(1 −0.2)3(1 −0.7)2 = 0.95.
The structure function for a scenario where B1 has failed can therefore be seen as a parallel arrangement of
the minimal series path structures and the structure function can becomes
ϕ(F) = ρ1 × ρ2 = 0.49 × 0.95 = 0.47.

==PAGE:79==
4 Approach for Bridge’s Structural System Design
Representing structural systems in block diagrams becomes more comprehensive when accounting for load
distribution. A less common, but yet simpler approach has been chosen to represent the structural system in
the following. The layout is a graphical representation of a so-called reliability network. The advantages of a
reliability network is that it highlights the load redistribution capacity and redundancy and also that the layout
of the structural system (e.i. the illustration of the reliability network) is comparable to the architectural draw-
ings. Furthermore, repetitions of failure mode or components, as observed for a block diagram, is eliminated
(Tharmabala & Nowak, 1987 Cremona, 2013). Section 4.1.1 covers the axiom of a reliability network while
establishing the reliability network for the implemented example.
4.1.1
Reliability network representation
The theory presents a procedure which simplifies the reliability study of complicated structural systems. In
structural reliability, the establishment of the structural system determines the structural resistance. The relia-
bility network allows a graphical representation of a system, which is more simple than a block diagram and
it asses the way of which a structure functions. The approach of establishing a structure function with regard
to a reliability network representation is addressed in the following.
For the graphs, consider a finite set S and the product set SxS. Let U be a subset of SxS; then the ordered
pair
G = (S, U)
(4.7)
is called a graph and the elements S are called vertices of the graph and the elements of U are pairs of vertices
are called arcs of the graph. r-fold graphs are defined as the graphs whereby two or more arcs exist between
two vertices. A reliability network R is defined in a set of e components consisting of:
• An r-fold graph G = (S, U) without loops and in which two vertices O ∈S and Z ∈S is distinguished
and called the origin and the end, respectively.
• A mapping ∆that corresponds to a component to each arc of the graph.
where r is the number of arcs having the same initial point and the same terminal point. Figure 4.4 presents
the above detonation example of a two span, two girder bridge, in a reliability network with the arcs and a
definition of the vertices of the graph (Kaufmann et al., 1977).
48
==PAGE:80==
4.1 Authentication of structure function
Figure 4.4: Reliability network system for a two-span composite two-girder bridge with definition of the arcs
and vertices.
The vertices S of the graph and U representing the pairs of vertices and the set of components e is:
S = {O, F, G, H, J, K, M, N, P, Z},
U = {(OF)A1, (OF)E1, FG, GH, HJ, (JZ)A3, (JZ)E4, (OK)B1, (OK)E1, KM, MN, NP, (PZ)B3,
(PZ)E4, FK, GM, HN, JP},
e = {A1, A2, A3, B1, B2, B3, E1, E2, E3, E4}.
To any subset e1 ⊂e of components, one may correspond to the partial graph Gp (e1) of the graph G,
obtained by retaining only the arcs of G, to which correspond a component belonging to e1 as
Gp(e1) = (S, Up(e1))
with
Up(e1) = {u ∈U|∆(u) ∈e1}
(4.8)
The structure function can be established by applying the theory regarding path and cuts as presented in Section
4.1. However, the path vector is referred to as a link when the approach concerns graphical representation.
A cut of a reliability network refers to a subset of arcs Up(b) contains a cut to the G relative to a subset of
vertices including O but excluding Z, where b = {ei, i ∈I}.
A link of a reliability network will refer to a subset a ⊂e of components, a = {ei, i ∈L}, such that there
exists in the graph Gp(a) a path from O to Z. To each link there can be more than one path of the graph.
Inversely, to a path µ = (u1, u2, ..., ul) from O to Z there corresponds a link a formed by the images of the
arcs u1, u2, ..., ul through the mapping ∆(Kaufmann et al., 1977; (Cremona, 2013)). For example, Figure
4.4 has the path µ = ((OF)E1, FG, GH, HJ, (JZ)E4) with the link a = {E1, A1, A2, A3, E3}. The same
vertices has a second path µ = ((OF)A1, FG, GH, HJ, (JZ)A3) with the link a1 = {A1, A2, A3}.
In order to establish the structure function, the minimal links and cuts are required. The definition of
minimal links and cut are similar to the minimal path and cut vector. The partial graph containing the fewest
49
==PAGE:81==
4 Approach for Bridge’s Structural System Design
components. Therefore, mapping of the minimal links becomes:
µ1 = ((OF)A1, FG, GH, HJ, (JZ)A3)
with
a = {A1, A2, A3},
µ2 = ((OK)B1, KM, MN, NP, (PZ)B3)
with
a2 = {B1, B2, B3}
The minimal links is seen to be equal the ones determined in Section 4.1. In order to establish the structure
function, the approach in previous section can be followed, assuming a = F.
When establishing the minimal link in a reliability network, the vertices are essential. In the case of a
component failure, the vertices S of the graph should survive. The first partial graph contained O, F, G, H, J
and Z, the second contained O, K, M, N, P and Z. This means that if component B1 fails, as minimum the
vertices O, K, M, N, P and Z should be part of the mapping. Since the pair of the vertices (OK)B1 and KM
is no longer in play, when B1 fails, the only component related to K is E1 and components having vertices
M is E2 and B2. The minimal path then becomes
µ2 = (OK)E1, KF, FG, GM, MN, NP, (PZ)B3
with
a2 = {E1, A1, E2, B2, B3}.
It is seen that the minimal links in case of failure are identical to the minimal paths for the comprehensive
structural block diagram in Figure 4.3, even though the reliability network in Figure 4.4 is more comparable
to the block diagram in Figure 4.2. The reliability network for the system can be drawn even more simple.
Instead of each arch representing one component, the components can be illustrated as a box, similar to Figure
4.2 and 4.3. Then the two arcs OFA1 and OFE1 can be presented as one arc that either requires the survival
of A1 or E1. The same goes for all the other scenarios of where two vertices have more than one arcs. The
reliability network can then be illustrated as in Figure 4.5.
Figure 4.5: Illustration of reliability network for a slab-on-girder two span bridge.
The reliability network in Figure 4.5 is almost identical to Figure 4.2, except for the fact that the compo-
nents of the system are presented on a graph. Figure 4.5 enables a more simple representation of the system
than Figure 4.2. It does not require the components to be illustrated more than ones in order to account for
the load distribution.

The passive redundancy has been presented in the structural system in Figure 4.5. The components or
substructure are in reserve meaning, that they are not expected to be used under normal circumstances, for
example a spare tire in a car.
4.2
Development of reliability network for bridge structures
The following present the characterization of three different bridge types along with establishment of their
structural systems presented by reliability network, to obtain more comprehensive illustrations, which is
preferable when preforming structural reliability assessment. The construction of the structural system does
not include the probabilistic notion. Altering the structural system into a structure function, the reliability of
structural systems can be evaluated, which include the probabilistic concepts (Thoft-Christensen & Murotsu,
1986, Ch. 2). The three bridge types investigated is first a truss bridge in Section 4.2.1, then a arch birdge
presented in Section 4.2.2 and final in Section 4.2.3 a cable bridge.
4.2.1
Truss bridge’s characterization and establishment of reliability
network
A bridge structure is characterized as a truss bridge when the load carrying superstructure consists of a truss.
In mechanics, a truss is typically described as a structure with connected two-force (either tension or com-
pression) members forming a triangle. This design of girder is entirely made of equilateral triangles. The
illustration in Figure 4.6 shows the terminology for a modified warren truss bridge. Each truss is composed
of an upper chord, a lower chord and several vertical and diagonal members.
Figure 4.6: Truss bridge terminology (Lin & Yoda, 2017, Fig. 8.3).
51
==PAGE:83==
4 Approach for Bridge’s Structural System Design
The two trusses are connected with a series of transverse members, lateral bracing, floor beams etc. The
top and bottom members acts like a simple beam, meaning that the upper part is in compression and the lower
part in tension. The vertical and diagonal elements can be in either compression or tension, depending on the
response to the dynamic loads.
In the process of applying structural reliability to establish the durability of the bridge, a breakdown of
the system to a number of n relevant components is required. Considering the truss bridge on Figure 4.6, the
structure can primarily be broken down to three substructures:
• The top chord system - consisting of the upper chords (longitudinal elements), the upper chord transverse
bracing (struts) and the upper chord bracing (the lateral bracing).
• The bottom chord system - consisting of the floor beams, the lower chords and associated lateral and
transverse bracing, the stringers and the deck.
• The web system - consisting of the vertical and diagonal members.
In the top chord system, the longitudinal upper chord elements are in compression. The lateral bracing con-
necting the chords provides lateral resistance. In addition to resisting externally applied transverse loads due
to example wind, lateral bracing stabilizes the compression chords. The lateral bracing ensures that reason-
ably small effective lengths are obtained for the truss members. The transverse bracing, also called struts,
are designed to help carry the transverse loads, prevent buckling and are connecting the upper chords. The
bracing is assumed not to have any essential impact on the structural survival, the top chord system therefore
consist of the upper chords element in a series system, see Figure 4.7. The critical failure is therefore assumed
to be failure of any upper chord element.
Figure 4.7: Structural system presented by a reliability network for the top chord substructure.
The web members are either vertical or diagonal. The diagonals carry the shear forces in the truss sections.
The verticals also provide both shear force capacities and additional loading supports for reducing the span of
chords. The webs members are acting in a series system.
The bottom chord system consists of the floor beam system, which is transverse to the traffic direction
connecting two trusses, and they are designed to support the loads from the stringers and transmit them to the
main trusses. The stringers are longitudinal beams under the deck, set parallel to the traffic direction. They
support the deck load directly and transmit them to the floor beams. In case of a truss bridge without stringers,
the deck is designed to be supported directly by the floor beams. The deck in a truss bridge is similar to
those in other bridge types. They directly support loads (vehicle loads or pedestrian loads etc.) and transmit
52
==PAGE:84==
4.2 Development of reliability network for bridge structures
them to the floor beams. Lateral bracing in truss bridges is provided for transmitting longitudinal live loads
to the bearings and also to prevent buckling for compression chords. This done by providing stringers, chord
lateral bracing or maybe even braking girders, the last one mentioned is not illustrated on Figure 4.6. In case of
highway bridges with a concrete cover deck, the deck will also act as lateral bracing support system. However,
the deck is not a critical failure, since the structure still would survive, even if the deck cover failed.
The lower chords are in a parallel system with the floor beams and the deck stringers. The structural
system for the floor system is shown in Figure 4.8. The lower chords elements are acting in series system,
with the bracing providing redundancy.
Figure 4.8: Structural system presented by a reliability network for the bottom chord substructures of the
modified warren truss bridge.
The structural systems for the three substructures upper chord system, web system and bottom chord
system are acting in a series system, if one of the substructures fails, the entire structural system fails. The
structural system for the modified warren truss bridge illustrated in Figure 4.6 is shown in Figure 4.9.
Figure 4.9: Structural system presented by a reliability network for the truss bridge.
The truss bridge is statically indeterminate, meaning that structural failure will not necessarily appear after
collapse of an element, but a relocation of the loads would more likely take place, leading to decrement of the
53
==PAGE:85==
4 Approach for Bridge’s Structural System Design
element’s reliability (i.e. higher probability of failure).
If a web member fails it is assumed that the forces will be transferred into the upper chord and bottom
chord, which means that the webs are in a parallel connection with the upper and bottom chord, see Figure
4.10.
Figure 4.10: Structural system presented by a reliability network for scenario of failed web.
This also means that the failure of the structure, after failure of an upper or bottom chord element, is
depended on the relocation of the forces into the webs, due to the connections point. This means that not only
does the reliability of the components change in case of web failure, but also the structure function, as shown
in Section 4.1, when establishing the system failure probability with a failed element. The illustration of the
system in a reliability network enables the possibility of a layout that accounts for these connections points.
The process of defining and representing the structural system in a reliability network, for a modified
warren truss bridge has been undertaken. However, truss bridges can have many different designs, for example
of the webs, but the designs can also be named according to the deck location. Figure 4.11 illustrates three
different deck locations for truss bridges
54
==PAGE:86==
4.2 Development of reliability network for bridge structures
Figure 4.11: Truss bridges with different deck locations. (A) Deck truss bridge. (B) Half-through truss bridge.
(C) Through truss bridge. (Lin & Yoda, 2017, Fig. 8.6).
Figure 4.11(A) shows a deck truss bridge where the deck is located at the top of the truss. This means
that vehicles and other live loads are carried above the top chords. Figure 4.11(B) illustrates a half-through
truss bridge, where the deck is located in the middle of the truss. In Figure 4.11(C) a through truss bridge is
shown, for this bridge structure the vehicles are passing through the truss. The previous establishing of the
reliability network was with regard to a through truss bridge, see Figure 4.9. The structural system is presented
differently for the three truss bridge types depending in the deck location. Figure 4.12 illustrates the reliability
network of a truss deck bridge.
55
==PAGE:87==
4 Approach for Bridge’s Structural System Design
Figure 4.12: Structural system presented by a reliability network for a deck truss bridge.
In Figure 4.12, it is seen that the stringer and beam system are in a parallel system with the upper chords.
When it comes to choosing between a through truss and a half-through truss bridge, the optimal decision is
made with regard to the span length, live load types, economical efficiency etc. It should be recognized that
the half-through truss bridge does require some extra provisions to resist lateral forces. The top chords is
designed as columns with elastic lateral support at truss connections. The critical buckling load should be
examined under design load (considering the impact factor for the live load) in order to grant stability.
4.2.2
Arch bridge’s characterization and establishment of reliability
network
An arch bridge is a structure where the shape of the load carrying system is an arch. The definition of an arch
is a curved structure that supports the loads parallel to its axis symmetry. An arch bridge works by transferring
the self-weight and other external loads acting in vertical directions partially into a horizontal thrust restrained
by the abutments at either side of the bridge. A notable quality of an arch bridge is the existence of horizontal
reactions at the end and relatively small bending moment at any sections. The bending moment caused by the
horizontal reactions is used to balance the bending moment due to vertical reactions and the dead load, live
load etc. An illustration of the terminology for a one-span arch bridge is presented in Figure 4.13.
56
==PAGE:88==
4.2 Development of reliability network for bridge structures
Figure 4.13: Arch bridge terminology (Lin & Yoda, 2017, Fig. 9.3).
Construction of arch bridges has occurred since ancient times, likely due to availability of stone masonry.
They have been built continuously through time, using stone, timber, cast iron, steel and concrete as building
materials. The use of cast iron to build arch bridges began in the end of the 18th century. However, in more
modern times and today, preferably materials for construction are reinforced concrete and structural steel.
They provide the opportunity for more slender arches and give the possibility of longer capacity.
For the establishment of the structural system for an arch bridge, Figure 4.13 is considered. In the illus-
tration it is shown that the deck is located above the arch, which is called a deck arch, and the components
between the arch and the deck are called the spandrel. In this specific case the spandrel consists of vertical
columns, which categorize the bridge as open braced spandrel. The arch bridge can be divided into three
subsystems;
• The floor system
• Vertical support
• The arch
For the floor system, the deck can be considered divided into elements at the critical sections, which are
the area between two vertical supportive columns. Even though the substructure appears to be in a series
system, the individual substructures is highly redundant. The floor system could consist of beam elements,
diaphragms or girders. The spandrel columns are behaving in a series systems also, if one column breaks, the
system fails. The arch is here considered solid (example stones), but if using prestressed concrete or girder,
higher redundancy would be provided. The structural system for the arch bridge is illustrated in Figure 4.14
57
==PAGE:89==
4 Approach for Bridge’s Structural System Design
Figure 4.14: Structural system presented by a reliability network for the arch bridge.
As for the truss bridge, the location of the deck can vary, called a deck arch, through arch and half-through
arch, which would result in the same changes in the structural system as for the truss bridge. However, designs
which provides higher redundancy to the spandrel system exist, like a braced spandrel arch bridge as seen in
Figure 4.15.
Figure 4.15: Illustration of braced spandrel arch bridge (Lin & Yoda, 2017 Fig. 9.13(F)).
The structural system for the arch bridge illustrated in Figure 4.15 will result for each column to be part
of a parallel system, since active redundancy is provided. The structural system for the entire structure is
illustrated in Figure 4.16.
Figure 4.16: Structural system presented by a reliability network for a braced spandrel arch bridge.
4.2.3
Cable bridge’s characterization and establishment of reliability
network
A cable-stayed bridge, also referred to as a cable bridge, is a structural system where the essential load sup-
ported and transferring members are the cable stays. The bridge structure of continuous girder, supported by
58
==PAGE:90==
4.2 Development of reliability network for bridge structures
the stay cables which are attached to the tower, as shown in Figure 4.17. From a mechanical point of view
a cable-stayed bridge is therefore a continuous girder bridge supported by elastic support. In cable-stayed
bridges, all members mainly works in either tension or compression. The cable stays are in tension, and the
towers (or pyloner) and main girder (or bridge deck) are in compression.
Figure 4.17: Example of a cable stayed bridge (Lin & Yoda, 2017, Fig. 10.1).
One of the initial designs of a cable-stayed bridge goes back to 1595. In the beginning of the 19th century,
a considerable number of cable-stayed bridges were built. However, absence of understanding these systems
resulted in several of the bridges to collapse. They were constructed in a manner that made it incapable
to tension the stays. These would become slack when effected by numerous load actions. Adjustment and
improvement regarding the inadequate resistance were not seen until the late 19th century. In the early 1950s,
the modern concept of the cable-stayed bridges were proposed. Some main problems that have been dealt with
were insufficient regarding the anchorage system, steel material and corrosion. In the 1950s, cable bridges
began to be more widely used and both design and construction of cable-stayed bridges have had a quicker
development than any other bridge type during this period.
In the process of developing the structural system, the cable stayed bridge can be decomposed to three
subsystems
• The tower system
• The cable-stayed system, consisting of the cables.
• The girder system
The towers or pylons are in series systems along with the end supports. However, because the pylons can
be built more economically and with higher redundancy, using reinforced or pre-stressed concrete, especially
large cable bridges are benefiting.
The vertical loads acting on the main girder are carried by the intermediate elastic support provided by
the stay cables. The support allows the girder to span longer distance than it would without the cable-stays.
The cables needs to sustain the tensile forces to carry the loads applied on the bridge deck. Each cable are
provided with a high level of redundancy. Cables in the same stay, so attached at the same tower at the same
59
==PAGE:91==
4 Approach for Bridge’s Structural System Design
side, are in a parallel system. However, each stay is in a series system, which means that reliability network
representing the cable-stayed system is a series system.
Since the cables are in tension, the main girder and towers are subjected to compressive forces. The girder
and towers are also subjected to bending moments and other forces, generally their effects are much smaller
then the axial forces. The structural system for the girder depends on the critical failure modes including the
number of girders. The failure modes are in a series connection in each girder, and the girders are in a parallel
system. For the cable bridge shown in Figure 4.17 the structural system is illustrated in Figure 4.18.
Figure 4.18: Structural system presented by a reliability network for the cable bridge.
In Figure 4.17, it is assumed that the cable system in the lateral direction is designed as a single plane
located above the center line, as seen in Figure 4.19(A). However, one of the more severe problems in relation
to a central cable plane system is its insufficient torsional stiffness to resists the twisting moment from eccentric
loading e.g. traffic load in only one side of the carriageway. If this is the case, a box-section for the floor system
may be used to achieve the required torsional stiffness. The layout of the cable stays effects the structural
behaviour, the erection method and the architectural expression. Figure 4.19 presents three different designs
for cable bridges, were the lateral direction for the cable system is shown in three different scenarios.
60
==PAGE:92==
4.3 Material properties for bridge constructions
Figure 4.19: Lateral cable arrangements. (A) One center plane (single plane). (B) Two lateral planes (double
planes). (C) Three planes (triple planes) (Lin & Yoda, 2017, Fig. 10.7).
Figure 4.19(A) shows a cable-stayed bridge with one plane cable system. Figure 4.19(B) presents a two
plane system. Both illustrations present the cable systems vertical, but the cables could also be inclined at the
edges of the girder. Figure 4.19(C) illustrates a three plane cable system with three cable systems, one attached
at the center line and one at each edge of the girder. The most common layout is a cable bridge constructed
with a two plane system. Compared to the single plane system, the two plane can provide necessary torsional
resistance for cable stayed bridges. In cases where a wide deck is required, it is possible to design three cable
planes because transverse bending moment is reduced when the deck can be divided into two parts, which is
accomplished designing three cable planes. Having more than one cable system in the lateral direction results
in the structural system to be in a parallel connection. This also effects the critical failure modes of the girder.
4.3
Material properties for bridge constructions
The materials properties of structural elements, effect the behaviour and as a results, the structural reliabil-
ity. The concept of Daniels systems, which take into account the material properties of parallel systems were
presented in Section 3.1.3.2. The Daniels system is presented for either perfectly ductile and brittle i.e. evalu-
ation of bridges’ probability assessment of parallel systems depends on the material properties. The reliability
networks presented in Section 4.2 illustrates that the three bridge constructions have a substructure at some
61
==PAGE:93==
4 Approach for Bridge’s Structural System Design
level where the components are quantified in a parallel system.
The main characteristics of ductile and brittle materials are that ductile materials allows for substantial
plastic deformation prior to fracture, whereas brittle materials fracture without deformation when their strength
is reached. Brittle materials have a high compression strength, but lack strength in tension or shear (Thoft-
Christensen & Murotsu, 1986, Ch. 5). Besides their contradiction, there are identical properties for brittle and
ductile materials, such as:
• Under tensile loading, both materials is identified with plastic deformation (Jensen et al., ? , Ch. 1).
• Ductility and brittleness is highly temperature dependent, the phenomenon is referred to as ductile to
brittle transition. A ductile material can convert into a brittle by decrease of temperatures and brittle
materials can behave ductile by increase of temperature. The temperature at which transition occur is
referred to as the transition temperature (Gerberich & Yang, 2003).
• The inbuilt stress level impact the ductility and brittleness of the material. A ductile material may fail
without evident plastic elongation under presence of high residual stress. The loss of ductility at places
of high stress concentration results in brittle behaviour (Chen & Duan, 2014, Ch. 1).
Traditional building materials for bridges are stone, timber, steel and concrete. Before availability of concrete
and steel, most bridges were made of the natural heterogeneous materials wood and stone (Lin & Yoda, 2017,
Ch. 3). Wood is an anisotropic element, with compressive strength, tensile strength and shear forces. The
properties depends on the load direction with respect to the grain directions. In general, wood is considered
mostly brittle. To satisfy demands of ductility metallic connections can be entrusted which are expected
to sustain large inelastic deformations while preventing collapse. The ductile behaviour of connections is
influenced both by metallic fasteners and by the strength properties of the wood surrounding the connection
zone (Concu, 2017).
Stone is also a brittle material often used for construction of arch bridges. Due to the high compression
strength of stones, the properties of brittle materials is beneficial in pure compression structures such as arch
bridges, see section 4.2.2.
In modern bridge constructions concrete and steel are the most adopted materials, concrete is mostly con-
sidered brittle2 and steel ductile. Brittle materials fracture when the strength is reached without any deforma-
tion, this is identified as a dangerous phenomenon since failure may occur without any prior warning which
from a safety perspective is considered unacceptable. This obstacle is comprehended by casting concrete with
example steel, the tensile properties will provide the cast element with ductile characteristics. Plain concrete
is not applied for construction purpose, here either reinforced concrete(RC) or pre-stressed concrete is used,
whereas the latter is more widely used for bridge structures.3 The benefit of prestressed concrete compared to
reinforced concrete is the ability to resist higher tensile stresses developed after inauguration during service.
2Concrete is considered quasi-brittle, since the material fracture mechanism result in dissipation prior to cracking with no or negligible
permanent strain
3Reinforced concrete can be developed using reinforcement steel, polymers or other composite materials. For prestressed concrete,
compressive stresses is induced by high-strength steel tendons before external loading is applied (Lin & Yoda, 2017, Ch. 3).
62
==PAGE:94==
4.4 Chapter conclusion on approach for bridge’s structural system design
Furthermore, prestressing allows for broader range of designs and to build lighter and shallower concrete
structures, without sacrificing the strength (Chen & Duan, 2014, Ch. 3; Lin & Yoda, 2017, Ch. 3)
Steel is also used for construction of bridges, it has relatively higher toughness or energy to failure than
concrete due to its ductility. The high energy absorption is beneficial with regard to damage resistance and is
suitable for long spans. Normally, ductile materials posses low strength properties, but this can be modified
to an extent via hardening techniques which means that steel can be lighter than concrete for the same load-
carrying capacity. Steel is beneficial in scenarios where long spans and high tension is required and the
material has a longer life-time. However, steel can be subjected to corrosion when exposed to air, water and
humidity, which means maintenance of protection is required. Furthermore, it has also shown small resistance
to fire compared to concrete (Chen & Duan, 2014, Ch. 1; Bonnerup , 2009, Ch. 1).
4.4
Chapter conclusion on approach for bridge’s structural
system design
In this chapter, it was described how the structural system can be converted into a structure function in order
to quantify and evaluate the reliability of a bridge system. In order to account for both the failure modes com-
bined effects and the geometry of the structure, the structural system reliability network was introduced and
executed for three bridge types, instead of the traditional block diagram approach. Examples and illustrations
of the a truss bridge, arch bridge and cable bridge is presented in a reliability network, including quantification
of the bridges. The advantages of a reliability network is that it highlights the load distribution capacity and
redundancy and also that the layout of the structural system (e.i. the illustration of the reliability network)
is comparable to the architectural drawings. Furthermore, repetitions of failure mode or component, as ob-
served for a block diagram, is eliminated. Furthermore is the material properties used in bridge constructions
discussed, since the material behaviour is essential with regard to the reliability analysis.
63
==PAGE:95==
==PAGE:96==
CHAPTER5
Applied Decision Analysis
using SIS for Bridge
Assessment
The previous chapters established the theoretical framework needed to classify and evaluate bridges in a re-
liability perspective. In this chapter, a case study on decision analysis of structural information is presented
and discussed. The theoretical framework developed in the previous chapters is used to asses the optimal
decision given a number of sources and classification of the bridge in terms of capacity. Firstly, the generic
probabilistic model for the structural system under consideration is presented. Next, the decision framework
illustrating additional information options and adaptive actions is presented in a decision tree. The probabilis-
tic characteristics of the structural system, assuming a Class 200 bridge as a starting point for the structural
decision modelling, is defined. The probabilities for the different performance system states are provided
with regard to the structural information sources and adaptive actions. The associated cost and income of
the bridge system and test methods are estimated, followed by deriving a service life integrity management
and risk model. A complete cost-benefit analysis is then developed, providing illustration of benefit models
of the prior decisions, along with presentation of the net income and benefits of all relevant strategy choices.
The expected life cycle revenue, net income and expenses related to cost and risk is tabulated, including a
risk assessment discussion the influence of implementing structural information sources related to the index
of vulnerability and robustness. Finally, value of information analyses with regard to the optimal benefits for
each option of test method is calculated and discussed. Finally, a recap and discussion section related to the
essential results of the case study is provided. The chapter ends with a conclusion on the case study.
5.1
Probabilistic model formulation
Firstly, a generic structural model for a perfect Daniels brittle system is presented. Secondly, the framework
for a subsequently pre-posterior decision analysis is provided. Thirdly, the specific modelling equations for
structural information sources relevant to this case study is presented and discussed.
65
==PAGE:97==
5 Applied Decision Analysis using SIS for Bridge Assessment
5.1.1
Structural model formulation
For a real-life structure, the process of formulation the structural model can be rather complex and computa-
tionally expensive. Therefore, the following aims to establish a structural model that can reasonably represent
the behaviour of a bridge structure or substructure. It needs to be adequate simple to be used in a decision
analysis and also in order to limit the extent of the work within relevance of the defined scope.
The formation of the limit state function for a component in a structural system follows the theory presented
in Section 3.1.1. For the structural model the system loading S is described with an extreme value distribution
with a reference period of one year in accordance with ISO 2394, (2015). The limit state function is expressed
as
gf,i = MR,i · Ri (t) −S × MS,
(5.1)
where MR and MS represent the model uncertainties for the resistance and load, respectively. R is the time-
dependent component resistance (Thöns, 2018b). The deterioration-induced resistance reduction can be mod-
elled as the reduction of the initial component resistance Ri,0 in dependency of the time-dependent damage
evolution Di (t) continuously with
Ri (t) = Ri,0 (1 −Di (t)) ,
(5.2)
where i is the component number i = 1..n, with n being the number of components. A generic and general
formulation of the degradation development is adopted, since the investigation does not include a specific
deterioration mechanism. Therefore, the generic degradation model presented in Section 3.1.5 is chosen with
Di (t) =
t
∑
k=1
∆Di,k,
(5.3)
including the annual increments ∆Di,k for the ith component at year k where k = 1..t, having the same
distribution with uncertain expected value MµD and constant standard deviation σ∆D (Qin et al., 2015).
The types of structural systems are described in Section 3.1.3. It is chosen to formulate the model as a
parallel system, since most bridges have a substructure that is statically indeterminate (redundant), see Section
4.2. Daniels systems accounts for the influence that material properties have regarding redundant systems,
see 3.1.3.2. Comparing two Daniels systems, one being perfectly brittle and the other perfectly ductile, but
otherwise considered identical with regard to the variables, then the highest failure probability is related to
the brittle system.1 The materials and the their properties with regard to ductility and brittleness utilized for
bridge construction is discussed Section 4.3. The most widely adopted construction materials are concrete
and steel, where concrete shows brittle behaviour and steel ductile.2 If a brittle system is satisfying the safety
1The only situation where a perfectly brittle system is not providing the largest failure probability, is when then the system is uncor-
related and the perfectly ductile system is fully correlated, see Section 3.1.3.2.
2The concrete used for construction is often reinforced concrete or pre-stressed concrete, such that the structural elements has both
ductility and brittleness. Assuming perfectly brittle behaviour is therefore conservative, see 4.3
66
==PAGE:98==
5.1 Probabilistic model formulation
requirements, then a ductile system will to. Therefore, it has been chosen to formulate a perfect brittle Daniels
system model, for a number of n components, since it is the most conservative design assumption.
For a perfectly Daniels brittle system, it follows that if the load S exceeds the system load bearing capacity,
the weakest component breaks first and the load is equally distributed among the remaining intact components.
The entire system fails when the remaining intact components cannot jointly carry the additional load fraction
that was sustained by the last component that broke. The limit state function for the structural system is given
by
gf,s = MR,i × max
1≤i≤n
(
(n −i + 1) ˆRi (t)
)
−n × S × MS,
(5.4)
where ˆRi (t) represents the ordered realizations of the constituent resistances according to ˆ
R1 (t) ≤... ≤ˆ
Rn (t)
(Thöns & Schneider, 2016). The probability of system failure can then be defined as
Pf,s = P [gf,s ≤0] ,
(5.5)
and the probability of failure for a component as
Pf,i = P [gf,i ≤0] .
(5.6)
The limit state functions account for the structure and components in ultimate limit state. The serviceability
limit state is when the structure or component is assumed unfit for its intended service. The serviceable state
encompass both irreversible and reversible state. For irreversible serviceability states, the degradation of a
structure or component results in permanent damage conditions, and repair or replacement is required in order
to restore satisfied functionality of the component or structure.
In some cases the limit state function can be expressed in terms of the deterioration D and the damage
capacity, whit a limit value of the damage resistance, which characterises the serviceability performance of the
component (e.g. deflection, stress). Nonetheless, this particular study requires that that the damage limit state
function, does not solely consists of the deterioration D and a limit value, since the generic deterioration model
incorporated is independent of the load parameters.3 Therefore, the probability of damage Pd is expressed
by a limit value related to the worsening Wlim > 0, hence Wlim is a critical value that sets boundaries for
the limit state function of failure, such that gf ≤Wlim. The damage limit state function for the irreversible
serviceability state can be expressed as
gd,i = MR,i × Ri (t) −S × MS −Wlim,
(5.7)
which also can be expressed as gd,i = gf,i −Wlim. The probability of damage for the irreversible service
limit state for a component is then
3Since the case study investigates capacity in terms of load model, the change in applied load S when reclassifying, should impact
the degradation.

Pd,i = P [gd,i ≤0] .
(5.8)
The limit state function for the system damage probability is
gd,s = MR,i × max
1≤i≤n
(
(n −i + 1) ˆRi (t)
)
−n × S × MS −Wlim,
(5.9)
which also can be expressed as gd,s = gf,s −Wlim. The probability of damage for the irreversible service
limit state for the system is then
Pd,s = P [gd,s ≤0] .
(5.10)
The presented equations establish the structural system and component’s performance state with regard
to time-dependent probabilities of failure and damage. The system model is formulated for a perfectly brittle
Daniels system.

5.1.3
Implementation of additional information
Different types of structural information sources can be incorporated in order to improve the probabilities of
damage and failure for a structure during its lifetime. In this case study, the additional informations in the
decision process are tests performed on the structural system in order to obtain information, by use of the
presented SIS in Section 3.5. The tests that will be implemented are SHM, proof load test and proof load
test with SHM. The strategy of ’no testing’ insinuate that no test of either the structure or the components
are orchestrated at any time from the beginning to the end of service life. However, testing does not include
the inspections that can occur during the service life, meaning that visual inspections or other inspections still
can be executed during the process. The pre-posterior probabilities for the structure at time t > 1 takes into
account the prior history of the structure, when having no testing.
5.1.3.1
Incorporation of SHM information
Structural Health Monitoring refers to monitoring that seeks to achieve knowledge of the integrity of in-service
structures on a continuous real-time basis. SHM can therefore be seen as a mean to reduce uncertainties. The
gained knowledge is used to reduce the uncertainties, since the information regarding the performance of the
structure relates to the epistemic uncertainties. The knowledge achieved through SHM information could be
subjected to aleatory uncertainties, which needs to be taken into consideration; this might limit uncertainty
reduction.
However, it should be accounted for that the knowledge achieved through SHM information could be sub-
jected to aleatory uncertainties, which might limit the uncertainty reduction. This is usually encompassed by
SHM uncertainties comprehending the epistemic and aleatoric uncertainties, due to the measurement process,
the measurement technology and human errors (Thöns, 2018b). Models utilized in engineering like analytical,
empirical and semi-empirical models are subjected to model uncertainties. The model uncertainties account
for model inherent simplifications and assumptions and are determined by means of measurements (JCSS,
2001). This implies that SHM data contains information about the mode uncertainties. Therefore, SHM in-
formation can be modelled taking basis in the probabilistic characteristics model uncertainties (Thöns et al.,
2015).
For an existing structure, regardless the time of construction, the process of establishing the model uncer-
tainties consists of a realization of the model uncertainties. Performing long-term load monitoring in conjunc-
tion with an extreme value analysis, may lead to SHM information having knowledge regarding the loading
model uncertainties. (Thöns, 2018b; Thöns & Schneider, 2016) This is due to the fact that assigning SHM on
a structure provides information about the model uncertainty realization subjected to SHM uncertainties. The
SHM strategy thus provides information about the model uncertainties on the individual component. Then the
expected probability of failure for a component is calculated with a dependency of realization of the model
uncertainty MS accounting for the SHM uncertainty U (Thöns et al., 2015) as
P shm
f,i
(ts) = P
[
MR,i × Ri (ts) −S × ˆ
MS × U ≤0
]
.
(5.16)

5.1.3.2
Incorporation of proof load test information
Proof load testing has mostly been applied for the purpose of demonstrating sufficient resistance reliability of
a structure. The provided information from proof load tests has traditionally been utilized by truncating the
lower tail of the probability distribution of the resistance of the structure at the value which corresponds to the
applied load intensity. However, this concept is of limited generality since the approach generally concerns
the reliability updating of one particular structure or component. For this reason, an approach which takes
basis in the Bayesian probability updating is used (Nishijima & Faber, 2007; Brüske & Thöns, 2017; Brüske
& Thöns, 2016).
As expressed in Section 5.1.2, the reliability-based method related to decision making is considered with
regard to updating annual probabilities for existing structures, i.e. the probability of failure for each year
considering prior loads. For a proof load test, the prior load history account for both the proof loads and
prior probabilities considering the reliability of existing structures. However, for simplicity the following is
only presenting the probabilistic model for assessment of the structure reliability with the load history solely
considering the load test. Therefore, the probabilistic model applied in the pre-posterior decision analysis is
P (F ∩Ipl) = P (Ipl|F) × P (F) ,
(5.20)
71
==PAGE:103==
5 Applied Decision Analysis using SIS for Bridge Assessment
where Ipl is the identification of the proof load being a success (Brüske & Thöns, 2017). In the situation where
the proof load test is performed at time tp during the service life, the limit state function for the survival of
proof load testing on a component is:
gpl,j (tp) = MR,j × Rj (tp) −Spl,
(5.21)
where Spl is the load applied during the test and j is the jth component in a set of proof load tested components
pl. Proof load tests should be designed with regard to the number of tested components and the applied test
load magnitude, in such a way that the successful result achieves an annual failure probability after testing that
satisfies the acceptance criteria. If several alternative test designs exist, the best combination of the number
of tested components, and the applied test load must be identified.
The year of proof load testing is denoted tp. The pre-posterior probability of component failure in year t,
with tp < t and the component is assumed prof load tested, i.e. i = j, is
P pl
f,i (t) = P [(gf,i( t )≤0) ∩(gpl,i( tp )> 0)] ,
(5.22)
with the system failure probability for a proof load test given as
P pl



(5.23)

P pl
