(5.25)
In the situation, where the proof load test has failed, it does not necessarily mean that the structure will
actually collapse. In practice the test can be stopped before physical destruction of the structure or a component
occur, when it is evident that the tested system is insufficient to sustain the test load planned. In this case study,
the proof of insufficient strength assumes that a reconstruction of a new structure/component is needed.
The proof load test is based on the prior information, i.e. if the proof load test is performed in a year during
the service life of a structure, it is based on the probability of survival in the previous years. Therefore, the
failure probability at year t, should encompass both survival of proof load test and survival of the structure
up to year t−1. The probability of damage at year t should encompass both survival of proof load test and
survival of the structure up to year t.
The identification of the proof load being unsuccessful is Ipl. The failure of the proof load test is established
as tp = t, meaning that the structure should survive both the traffic load and the test load applied at the end
72
==PAGE:104==
5.2 Descriptive illustration of reliability based capacity enhancement
of the same year, including the prior traffic load history. The damage and failure probabilities with regard to
a successful identification, is therefore very similar to the Equations (5.22)-(5.25), except for the fact that the
all limit state functions now should be > 0
5.1.3.3
Incorporation of proof load test with SHM
The third test option is the combination of a proof load test with SHM. The installation of the SHM system,
takes place at the same year as the proof load test. The test load is based on a required load, that is needed
to insure that the annual probability of failure achieves the required annual failure of probability after proof
load testing, that satisfies the acceptance criteria. The realization of the model uncertainty MS and accounting
for the SHM uncertainty, as seen in Section 5.1.3.1 are implemented in the total load applied during testing
Spl consisting of both the permanent load and test load. The limit state function gpl−shm are then following
the same approach as for the Equations (5.22)-(5.25), however, instead of the limit state function of the prior
system, the limit state function regard to SHM is incorporated.
The purpose of applying both test methods is to investigate the possibility of further improvement, given
that the SHM system assists in obtaining information regarding the uncertainties related to traffic load to the
end of service life and proof load estimates the resistance. The cost of having both test methods can influence
the outcome of the final utilized benefit negatively, if the cost of executing both methods outrank the benefits
gained. This stress the importance of performing a cost-benefit analysis that takes the cost of buying and
implementing the test methods into account. If a test load is relatively sizeable a high failure probability of
the proof load test can occur, which can have tremendous negative impact on the structural survival. However,
a modest test load might not provide enough information regarding the capacity in order to improve the updated
probabilities compared to those prior to the proof load test.
5.2
Descriptive illustration of reliability based capacity
enhancement
The previous sections have presented the framework for the case study. Further details and assumptions are
required in order to provide a descriptive example for reliability assessment of a bridge structure including
pre-posterior decision analyses of structural information sources.
Various bridge types and their associated structural systems in the form of a reliability network is pre-
sented in Section 4.2. However, establishing the reliability of a real bridge is a complex and computational
costly solution, and many conditions need to be taken into consideration. The required analysis is generally
very comprehensive and contains probabilistic modelling of the capacity, loads and influences from the en-
vironment, relevant deterioration processes, uncertainties related to the applied models, and data assemble
approach. Therefore, a simple structural system that can present either the main system or a subsystem is
chosen to define the structure under consideration.
73
==PAGE:105==
5 Applied Decision Analysis using SIS for Bridge Assessment
5.2.1
Decision framework
A bridge deteriorate over its lifetime and it is important that it can fulfil its requirements during its intended
service life. Using structural information sources during the service life of a structure might lead to obtain
better and more precise information regarding the uncertainties. Even if a bridge is proven to satisfy its
requirements, test methods can still provide an economical benefit. The decision framework for this case is
presented in Figure 5.2, illustrated by the use of a decision tree.
Figure 5.2: Decision framework for the pre-posterior decision analysis of case study example.
The mathematical approach for the establishment of the time-variant system performances with regard to
consecutive pre-posterior analyses and the choice of strategy is presented in Section 5.1.
The actions linked with information acquirement strategy and the random outcome of the information,
varies depending on the test being a success or not. For a successful test, the actions are either kept at the initial
class, i.e. Class 200, changing to a different load class or construction of a new structure. The purpose is to
74
==PAGE:106==
5.2 Descriptive illustration of reliability based capacity enhancement
classify the bridge with regard to the maximum traffic load it can sustain while achieving its acceptance criteria.
Even though, the structure is resulting in failure and damage probabilities decrease of the system performance
when reducing the load class, the action choices related to changing the load class, is only relevant until
the point where the maximum load class that fulfil the requirements is obtained. The additional information
consists of three options: either applying SHM, performing a proof load test or performing a proof load test
with installation of a SHM system. Even though a proof load test would most likely be interrupted before
complete failure of the bridge, this study assumes that failure leads to replacement of the old structure.
5.2.2
Probabilistic characteristics of the structural system
.
The structure aims to be simple and manageable, yet representative. Therefore, a number of five parallel
components (i.e. n = 5) made from linearly elastic perfectly brittle material, is chosen to formulate the
structural system. Figure 5.3 show the structural system presented in a reliability network.
Figure 5.3: Illustration of the case study structural system with n = 5 components in a parallel connection
presented in a reliability network.
In order to establish the probabilities required in the decision analysis, the probabilistic models required
in the structural system limit state functions need to be established.
The reclassification is based on the traffic load the bridge capacity can sustain, following the approach
presented by Scholten et al., (2004). The traffic load is the changing variable when choosing the action of
changing the load class. In this section, the magnitude of the vehicle loads for the different classes are first
presented. Hereinafter follows the presentation of the remaining probabilistic model characteristics.
The load S is often a combination of several loads that need to be considered (CEN, 2007). For simplifica-
tion, the load S is here presented by a permanent load Sp and a variable load Sq. The variable load represent
the traffic load. Since most highway bridges are designed in Class 200, the starting point of investigation for
the prior probabilities related to the strategy of no testing, related to classifying the bridge as Class 200 with
the load S200 = Sp + Sq,200 (O’Connor & Enevoldsen, 2009). As mentioned in Section 3.2, the estimation
of traffic load is based on the standard vehicle weight Wi, where i = 50, 60, .., 200. The yearly number Ni
is the standard vehicles crossing a highway bridge. The variable traffic load is expressed as a dynamic load
75
==PAGE:107==
5 Applied Decision Analysis using SIS for Bridge Assessment
modelled as a poisson distribution in works of (Scholten et al., 2004). The traffic load incorporates along with
the normal distributed vehicle load, knowledge regarding the transverse and longitudinal location in lanes, the
dynamic impact load, etc. (O’Connor & Enevoldsen, 2009). In consequence of the numerous information
required, simplifications are formed by assuming that the traffic load is equal to the vehicle loads.
Table 5.1 presents the yearly number N, mean value, and standard deviation of the heavy vehicles related
to the respective classes.

The permanent load can be assumed to be normally distributed with a coefficient of variation at 5% (Scholten
et al., 2004).
The load models and their uncertainties are considered uncorrelated, but the deterioration of structural
elements within a system is generally correlated due to common factors, such as environmental conditions and
materials characteristics (Schneider et al., 2014). The correlations is considered linear and the co-variance
matrix is established in order to account for the correlations. For the deterioration, the correlation is assumed
to be ρ (∆D) = 0.6. The correlations of the resistance and model uncertainties is ρ (R) = ρ (MR) = 0.5. The
model uncertainties MR and MS are determined by means of measurements (JCSS,2001).

damage limit satisfies the target reliability criteria for serviceability limit state, assuming low relative cost
safety measures, applying βt,sls = 2.6, since 2.6 > 2.3.
The probability models for the testing methods, can now be defined before the establishing of the proba-
bilities. The SHM approach consists on monitoring the extreme loading and is modelled in the realization of
the loading model uncertainty, accounting for the SHM uncertainties. Information about the extreme bridge
loading influences the uncertainties on a component and system level (Thöns et al., 2017). The SHM uncer-
tainty is modelled through a normal distributed random variable U = Us ∼N (µu, σu), where µu is a random
variable itself accounting for the statistical uncertainties due to the limited number of yearly observations no,
µu ∼N (µ, σ/√no).

load and the permanent load. Therefore, the load when performing proof load test is Spl = S′
pl + Sp × Ms.
The test load magnitude differs, for the different course of action, while the permanent load Sp remains the
same. The permanent load was derived previously in this section and is presented in Table 5.2.

5.2.3
Probabilistic modelling results and discussion
The probabilistic models and design conditions given in Section 5.2.2 are implemented in the equations pre-
sented in Section 5.1.1 for a Daniels brittle system. For the purpose of establishing the subsequently proba-
bilities and the pre-posterior probabilities with regard to obtaining additional information with yet unknown
results, the mathematical approach presented in Section 5.1.2 and Section 5.1.3 is followed, respectively. The
process is carried out in agreement with the decision framework presented in Section 5.2.1, in order to com-
pute the outcome of the probabilities of the system performance. Since the bridge is investigated over its
service life by a sequential decision analysis, the analysis contains the information gathered in previous time
steps, following the approach in Section 5.1.2, which presents the sequentially implementation of a decision
78
==PAGE:110==
5.2 Descriptive illustration of reliability based capacity enhancement
tree. The assigned test methods, i.e. the additional information, follows the approach presented in Section
5.1.3. A service life of 100 years is used in the computation since this is the expected service life of a bridge
construction in Europe (CEN, 2007, Tbl. 2.1). In the design phase described on the previous pages, the target
reliabilities were determined based on the assumptions that the relative cost of safety measures are small, and
that the consequences of failure are large. Therefore, the initial resistance of a component was calibrated to
be 590.92 in such a manner that the target reliability of 4.7 for a one year reference period with regard to the
ultimate limit state was fulfilled. The damage resistance for the serviceability limit state was calibrated to be
131.51 in order to fulfil the target reliability of 2.6. After the inauguration of the system, the relative cost of
safety measures becomes large and consequences of failure remain large. For a one year reference period this
results in target reliabilities of 3.7 and 1.3 for the ultimate and serviceability limit states, respectively. The
probability results are provided in Appendix A.
5.2.3.1
Initial probabilities
The initial probabilities for the bridge structure is computed based on the assumptions that the bridge is de-
signed and classified as Class 200. The following investigate and asses the initial probabilities, before con-
sidering any adaptive actions linked with information acquirement strategy and the random outcomes of the
information.
In order to represent the annual critical failure probabilities of the components, it is chosen to illustrate
the time-variant failure probabilities of component 4 because it endures the largest failure probability at end
of the service life, i.e. weakest component at year tsl = 100, see Table A.1 in Appendix A.1.1.1.

Firstly, in order to compare the computed results with literature, the probability failure rate over the service
life is investigated. As referenced in Chapter 2, a study of Schneider et al., (2014) presents system failure of
a bridge considering spatial deterioration.4 The results of the prior system failure is obtained from Fig. 10 in
Schneider et al., (2014) to approximately Pf,s (20) = 5.9 × 10−4 and Pf,s (50) = 5.7 × 10−2 corresponds to a
4In Schneider et al., 2014 Fig. 10, the system failure for the collapse mechanism of the structure is almost unchangeably for the
first 15 years, then a step increase until year 20 is reported. Here on after, the probability of failure continuously increases until the
end of service life at year 50. In Fig. 10, the steep increase observed in the early service life can be explained by the influence of the
deterioration mechanism and structural system. This is not observed for this study in Figure 5.4 due to the differences of deterioration
mechanisms and component interactions for the studies. Therefore, the comparison is made from year 20 and to the end of service life,
so a 30 year long period.
80
==PAGE:112==
5.2 Descriptive illustration of reliability based capacity enhancement
slope5 of approximately 0.066. Comparing this with a span of 80 for the current study, from year 20 to 100, due
to the different span of service life the slope obtained from Schneider et al. should be approximately 2.67 times
smaller, resulting in a slope of 0.024. In this case study the system probability failure is Pf,s (20) = 3.72×10−6
an Pf,s (100) = 6.09 × 10−4 with the slope of 0.027. The slope in this case study is approximately 11.54%
of the compared works of Schneider et al., 2014. Therefore, the failure probability rate is acceptable, since
the comparable does have some margin of error. The failure probability of this case study seems reasonable
compared to the study of Schneider et al.
Secondly, the failure probability rate is further compared to the probability of failure for a brittle Daniels
system. The study of Thöns et al., 2017 investigates the system failure probability for a wind park modelled
as a brittle Daniels system with a fatigue deterioration model over the service life of 25 years.6 The proba-
bility of failure at the beginning year is reported with Pf (1) = 3 × 10−4 and at the end of service life with
Pf (25) = 5 × 10−2, which result in a slope of 0.092. This service life span in this case study is 4 times longer,
which means that a slope 0.023, should be compared to the current work. For the structure in this study the
probability of failure increases with an ratio of 0.027 over the entire service life, with Pf,s (1) = 1.43 × 10−6
and Pf,s (100) = 6.09 × 10−4. The slope is approximately 19.58% higher compared to the works of Thöns et
al., (2017). The slope in this case study shows a higher probability failure rate compared to the those obtained
from the investigated work.
Thirdly, with regard to the ultimate limit state, the acceptance criteria for the design phase and after in-
auguration should be fulfilled with reasonable confidence.7 In Figure 5.4, it can be seen that the criterion is
satisfied for over half the service life of the structure. This is found adequate, because if the bridge is exposed
to greater damage by environmental attacks or increased traffic load higher than expected, it is a reasonable
assumption that over many years these effects results in an unacceptable state before the end of service life.
Finally, the relation between the probability of failure for a system component and the system itself is
analysed. In this case study, the failure probability of the system is found to be larger than the failure probability
of a component, for the circumstance of the system consisting of 5 components with coefficient correlations
ρ(R) = ρ (MR) = 0.5. In the works of Gollwitzer & Rackwitz, 1990, Fig. 5 shows that with a number
of 5 components in a brittle Daniels system, the failure probability of the system is larger than the failure
reliability for a component regardless of the correlation coefficient magnitude.8 The same is observed in
Brüske & Thöns, 2016 Fig. 7 left. Here, the reliability for a brittle Daniels system with resistance correlation
ρ (MR) = 0 does not change the system’s reliability mentionable under the condition of constant number
of components when adjusting the resistance coefficient correlation.9 In both of the referred studies, the
5The slope for a log-linear plot is calculated as m = logn (y2/y1)

system reliability is approximately 75% of the component reliability, regardless of the resistance coefficient

is increasing when the resistance correlation ρ (R) is increasing. Even though this case study enumerates a
system reliability at year 100 that results in approximately 90% of the reliability for component 4, this result is
considered plausible due to the impact that the coefficient correlation of the model uncertainties greater than 0
can have on the system reliability, as observed in the studies of Brüske & Thöns, 2016. This correspondence
strengthens the confidence in the computational framework developed for this case study with regard to failure
probability rate, even though the compared works of Schneider et al., (2014) and Thöns et al., (2017), has
a lower failure probability rate, but since the current case study investigates a perfectly Daniels system with
high correlation, higher failure probability would be expected.
These discussions indicate a high confidence in the computational framework developed for this case study.
Therefore, it is decided to proceed with the computational framework of the generic model in the following.
After having concluded that the framework for the generic model provides reasonable results, the next
step is to investigate the acceptance criteria assessment. The underlying data for Figure 5.4 indicates that the
criterion for failure probabilities of the system is satisfied until year 62. The probability of failure is calculated
to be Pf,s (62) = 1.07 × 10−4 (β = 3.7) for the year before the insufficient limit is reached. At year 63, the
probability of failure is Pf,s (63) = 1.16×10−4 (β = 3.68), resulting in a unacceptable probability of failure for
the bridge system. The acceptance criteria assessment of the ultimate limit state is related to the system failure.
The failure probability of the component at the end of service life is Pf,4 (100) = 1.96 × 10−4 (β = 3.55).

As part of the initial acceptance criteria assessment, the damage probabilities are also provided in order
to investigate the criterion with regard to the serviceability limit state. The plots in Figure 5.5 illustrates the
damage probability of the system and component 4, i.e. the weakest component, accompanied by the yearly
target probability Pf = 9.68 × 10−2 (βt,sls = 1.3). The probability of damage for the weakest component is
satisfied with regard to the target probability until year ts = 77, with the probability of damage Pd,4(77) =
9.48 × 10−2 (β = 1.31) and is deemed unfit at year ts = 78 with Pd,4(78) = 9.69 × 10−2 (β = 1.29). The
probability of the damage of the system is within the requirement, having the maximum probability of damage
Pd,s(100) = 9.01 × 10−3 at the end of service life. ,
The data illustrated in Figures 5.4 and 5.5 indicates that some kind of action is required in order to maintain
fulfilment of the acceptance criteria. In order to keep the bridge in service at the required safety level for the
rest of the service life, action must be taken no later than year 62. Furthermore, if initiatives are taken too late
or too early during the service life, the benefits of the chosen strategy may not outweigh the expenses related
to the specific strategy. Therefore, it is decided to investigate the optimal decision associated with the life
cycle benefits of the structure strategies, taken initiatives, when the structure is half way through its service
life, at the end of year 50, resulting in updated probabilities at year 51.

5.2.3.2
Probability of failure for all strategies
In the previous section it was established that initiatives are taken at year 51, when the structure is half way
through its service life. Figure 5.2 in Section 5.2.1 presented the different decision options in a decision
framework with the adaptive action linked with information acquirement strategy and the random outcomes
of the information. Specifically for this decision framework, the adaptive actions consists of classifying the
bridge to a specific load class or construct a new.10
As discussed in Section 5.2.1, the classification is associated with the highest vehicle load the bridge can
sustain under the conditions that the acceptance criteria is fulfilled. Therefore, the procedure of investigating
load classes starts with the initial class (i.e. Class 200) and continues with computation of the next nearest
class (i.e. Class 175), until the acceptance criteria is fulfilled. The following presents the acceptance criteria
assessment with regard to the ultimate limit state.11
Figure 5.6 presents the failure probability of the system from year ts = 50 to the end of service life with
regard to the relevant choice of action linked to the outcome of the different additional information and the
strategy of no testing. Figure 5.6 illustrates the annual time-variant failure probability for the system together
with the annual target probability for the ultimate limit state.12

advantageous gained by changing to load Class 150. Already at year 53 the probabilities of Class 150 with no
additional information increases the probabilities linked to a proof load test with SHM of a Class 200 bridge.

probability development rate in the first few years, result in this test option to be more profitable at the end of
service life than the choice of classifying to Class 150. At year 89, the failure probabilities for a proof load

The previous investigation with regard to the component failure stated that the traffic load had the greatest
impact regarding improvement of the failure development. Similar tendency is observed for the damage on
component level in Figure 5.8. Herein, all the strategies related to the action of having Class 200 (with the
construction of a new structure being an exception) provides the largest damage probabilities at the end of
service life and the greatest damage development rate throughout the service life. The action of changing to
Class 175, linked with the strategy of no testing compared to Class 175 with SHM and a proof load test, shows
that the strategy involving SHM results in the lowest damage probability. Therefore, knowledge regarding
traffic load is seen to have a greater impact on component damage level than knowledge regarding capacity
obtained from a proof load test of a Class 175 structure.
It is observed that the damage on component level for a new structure might exceed the damage probability
of Class 150. Therefore, the action of a lower class load might become more beneficial than reconstruction.
The load has a greater impact on the damage development rate than the deterioration. Therefore, the traffic
load magnitude is seen to have a greater impact with regard to component damage.
In Figure 5.8, it is found that the damage probability of proof load with SHM for Class 200 is larger than the
probabilities of proof load for Class 200. Therefore, the installation of SHM with the proof load is not more
90
==PAGE:122==
5.2 Descriptive illustration of reliability based capacity enhancement
sufficient on component damage level. However, it is important to remember that the component damage
depends on the survival. A higher component failure probability for proof load test of Class 200 was found
in Figure 5.7, compared to Class 200 for a proof load test with SHM. Therefore, installation of SHM can still
provide an improvement on the overall component level since it might be better to have higher probability of
damage, if this is related to lower probability of failure.
Both the system and the component failure probabilities are lower load Class 175 with SHM than the
action of changing to load Class 150 without any testing. However, in Figure 5.8 the damage probability
of Class 175 with SHM is found to be higher than damage probability of Class 150. This is consistent with
the statement that the SHM system might not have such considerable influence on the component damage
probability compared to failure probability of the system and the component.

5.2.4
Associated revenue and cost to the bridge system and tests
The probabilities of the system performance are related to revenue, cost, and consequences. An establishment
of these values are necessary in order to carry out a cost benefit analysis, risk assessment and VoI analysis, as
described in Chapter 3.3 and 3.4. The following describes the establishment of the revenue associated with
93
==PAGE:125==
5 Applied Decision Analysis using SIS for Bridge Assessment
the different load classes, costs related to the system performance, and costs related to purchasing additional
information and expenditure related to performance of the equipment. Costs and revenues are established
with respect to obtained fundamental information related to the construction and maintenance of the Great
Belt Bridge (Danish: Storebæltsbroen) in Denmark.
The Great Belt Bridge is chosen as a reference point in order to achieve a realistic ratio of the revenue and
the expenses. However, it should be stressed that this case study is not based on the Great Belt Bridge in any
way, it is only for the mere purpose of establishing representative monetary values used in further analyses.
5.2.4.1
Cost related to system performance
In order to establish the cost of the structural failure, it is essential to know the cost of rebuilding a new bridge.
The Great Belt Bridge was opened in 1998. The expense cost of constructing the bridge at that time was 21.4
billion DKK (A/S Storebælt, 2020b). The information regarding bridge revenue is seized from the annual
report from 2017, which is hereinafter established as the beginning year of the structural system. Therefore,
it is relevant to consider the cost of construction at year 2017. According to Statistics Denmark, the total
inflation from 1998 to 2017 was 40.3% (DST, 2019), which is equal to a constant inflation at 1.8 per year.
This results in the expense of constructing the bridge in 2017 to be 30 billion DKK. The in-service failure
of bridges may cause severe accidents involving the vehicles on the bridge at the instant of failure and also
have further costs associated to environmental damages and impact on general public. Bridge structures are
in consequence class 3 (i.e. CC3), see Section 3.2 Table 3.1. The ratio between the total cost of construction
plus direct failure cost and construction cost is between 5 and 10 (JCSS, 2001). A ratio of 9 is chosen based
on the assumptions that the modelled highway bridge serves a high number of vehicles, and system failure
will have high effects on the road network system.
The monetary values are obtained by dividing the values with the amount of 5,455,000 DKK. Table 5.8
presents the costs related to system performance in monetary values together with a description of the vari-
ables.
Table 5.8: Cost related to system performance in monetary units Mu.
Description
Variable
Monetary unit [Mu]
Cost of new structure
Cf,s
5500
Cost of structure failure in-service
Cf,in
49,500
Cost of failure of a component
Cf,i
1100
Cost of damage of the structure
Cd,s
275
Cost of damage of a component
Cd,i
55
Since the structural system consists of 5 components, the cost of a component failure Cf,i is assumed to
be 1/5 of the cost related to system failure Cf,s. The repair cost associated with damage of the structure or
a component is taken as 5% of the associated failure cost following Thöns et al., 2015. The repair cost of
94
==PAGE:126==
5.2 Descriptive illustration of reliability based capacity enhancement
damage for bridge structure can generally be assumed to be up to 10% of the failure cost (Khan, 2015). The
income of the structure is associated with the payment involving vehicle types crossing the bridge. If the
bridge is classified to a lower class than the initial, then restrictions are made regarding vehicle types that can
be granted permission to operate on the bridge. The revenue related to different classification classes will be
further discussed in the following section.
5.2.4.2
Cost and revenue related to classification class
The following presents the cost and revenue for the different classification classes related to the maximum
vehicle load the bridge can sustain. The revenue for the bridge is associated with the number of vehicles
crossing the bridge. A lower classification results in a decrease of truck types that can pass the bridge hence,
a decrease in revenue. In 2017, the total number of different vehicles passing the bridge were 12,779,645(A/S
Storebælt,A/S Storebælt), resulting in a revenue of 3.557 billion DKK (Sund & Bælt Holding A/S, 2018).
The observation of big trucks passing the bridge in 2017 was 1,210,073. Assuming that the number of big
trucks covers all trucks from Class 50 to Class 200, the number of standard vehicle types for a highway bridge
for Class 50 up to Class 200 is 1200 (Scholten et al., 2004, Tbl. 5.4). The number of standard vehicles is
used in order to estimate the percentage distribution among classes. This percentage is then adopted for the
purpose of estimating the number of trucks belonging to each class. Table 5.1 in Section 5.2.2 provides the
number of standard vehicles, N, crossing a highway bridge from Class 100 to Class 200, the total number is
300, leading to 25% of the trucks in the category big trucks, belonging to Class 100 up to 200. Furthermore,
besides the category big trucks, there is a category of trucks above 20 meters with the total number of 94329
trucks observed in 2017 (A/S Storebælt,A/S Storebælt). Formulating the assumption that no trucks above 20
meter would belong to any class lower than Class 100, meaning that 100% of the trucks are in the investigated
classes. Table 5.9 provides the number of vehicle types for each class from Class 100 to 200, related to the
categorisation of the observations. The assumed percentage of trucks belonging to each class is established
based on the number of standard vehicles N.

5.2.4.3
Cost associated with testing
The different test methods are associated with expenses. The proof load test is preformed ones during the
service life and has expenses related to the testing system. The SHM on the other hand has expenses that
96
==PAGE:128==
5.2 Descriptive illustration of reliability based capacity enhancement
continues from the day of installation and throughout the service life. The costs are related to installation and
investment expenses, where it is assumed that the monitoring equipment needs to be replaced every 10 year.
Furthermore, the SHM is associated with a yearly cost of operation. Table 5.12 shows the cost of additional
information for the test methods.
Table 5.12: The costs of additional information.
Description
Variable
Monetary unit [Mu]
Investment of SHM per component
CInv,shm
27.5

CInst,shm
27.5

Ci,Op,shm
0.22
Investment of proof load test
CInv,pl
55
The values are estimated on the background of related literature. In Thöns et al., 2017 the expenses of
investment and installation corresponds to 2.5% of the turbine investment. In this case study, the investment
and installation cost with regard to the SHM system is established as 2.5% of the component investment,
CInv,shm = CInst,shm = 27.5 Mu. In the same publication is the cost of operation 0.1% of turbine investment.
Adopting this assumption gives the operation cost COp,shm = 1.1, which means that the cost of operation for
each component is Ci,Op,shm = 0.22 Mu. In Brüske & Thöns, 2016 the investment cost of a proof load test
is 1% of the construction cost. This results in the investment of proof load test CInv,pl = 55 Mu, since the
construction cost Cf,s = 5500 Mu.
5.2.5
Service life integrity management and risk model
The service life integrity management model builds upon the reliability based strategy planning decision rule
with the adaptive actions. The expected revenues, costs, and direct and indirect risks are established. The prob-
abilities of the different system states presented in Section 5.2.3 are related to revenue, costs, and consequences
as shown in Table 5.13. In the risk assessment, consequences regarding human safety and environment are
accounted for in the cost of in-service failure.

The expected life cycle benefits are the sum of expected cost of the service life integrity management and
risks. Direct risk accounts for the risk of component and system damage as well as component failure. Indirect
risk is the consequences of system failure, where the cost takes into account the human health and life etc.
All monetary values are discounted to present value, i.e. to the value at the time investigated. The values
are discounted considering a generic discount rate of 5%. Proceedings are possible after year 50, meaning no
other strategy or any adaptive actions are initiated before year 51. Therefore, the initial starting time for the
cost-benefit analysis, can be set at year 50, hence td = t −50. The risks are established on the probabilities
presented in Section 5.2.3 and the consequences determined in Section 5.2.4.

5.2.6
Pre-posterior decision analysis
In the previous sections the methodology of service life integrity management and values of revenue, cost,
and risk were established. The computation of the subsequently annual probabilities for the different course
of actions linked with the information acquirement strategy were established an presented in Section 5.2.3.
Based on this ground work from these sections a pre-posterior decision analysis is now conducted based on
Bayesian decision theory presented in Chapter 3.3.
Firstly, a cost-benefit analysis is performed in order to investigate the values of the expected revenue,
cost and risks, together with net income and benefits of the structural system, see Section 5.2.6.1. The cost
and risks are categorized into two subcategories, the cost and risks obtained only from the structural system
performance and the cost and risk related only to the test method. The optimal expected life cycle benefits are
evaluated for with regard to different strategies.
Secondly, a risk assessment based on the theory described in Chapter 3.4 is presented investigating the
influences of the direct and indirect risks and the vulnerabilities and robustness of the structural system in
Section 5.2.6.2.
Thirdly, a value of information (VoI) analysis with regard to the pre-posterior life-cycle benefits is per-
formed in Section 3.3.4 in order to establish the optimal decision.
Finally, decision and recap of the final results for the decision analysis process is provided in Section
5.2.6.4. The section further evaluates different example scenarios were the obtained results are analysed.
The results is shown for a period of 50 years, where the initial starting time at year 0 is equivalent to the
probabilities half way through the service life at year 50. Consequently, the end of service life tsl is now taken
to be 50 years in the following instead of the 100 years used until now.
As discussed earlier in Section 5.2.4.2 a lower classification class and reduced capacity leads to a decrease
in revenue, since revenue in this case study depends on the number of trucks that can pass the bridge. This
correlation underpins the analysis in the following.
5.2.6.1
Cost benefit analysis
The cost benefit analysis is performed by inserting the computed results of the system performance proba-
bilities presented in Section 5.2.3 and the cost and revenues established in Section 5.2.4 into the equations
presented in Section 5.2.5. The approach follows the pre-posterior analysis in normal form, see Section 3.3.3.
First, the prior benefits are calculated and presented for all the considered actions linked with no testing, even
those established unacceptable with regard to the limit state criteria. The purpose is to inspect the influence
that reclassification or reconstruction of a Class 200 bridge has with regard to the expected revenues, costs and
risks. Hereafter, the net income of the accepted decision strategies with regard to additional information is
102
==PAGE:134==
5.2 Descriptive illustration of reliability based capacity enhancement
presented, in order to examine the influence that the additional information has on the structural performance,
without accounting for the expenses related to test method. Finally, the optimal prior life-cycle benefit and the
life-cycle benefits related to additional information are presented. The optimal life-cycle benefit with regard
to each individual test method, see Figure 5.2, is established.
Figure 5.10 illustrates the prior life cycle benefit B0 linked with no additional information, for the actions
of keeping the initial classification class, Class 200, reconstructing or reclassifying to Class 175 or Class 150.

In Figure 5.10, the benefits over the service life can be seen for the prior decisions, i.e. by definition
adaptive actions linked with no additional information. A benefit decrease in year 1 compared to year 0 is
observed when constructing a new bridge due to the expenses related to reconstruction cost at year 1. This is
the only action whereby a benefit decrease is found during the service life. This is not observed for any of the
other actions since there is no direct cost associated with reclassifying (only a change in revenue).
The probabilities of all four system states performance, associated with either damage or failure reduces,
when decreasing the load class magnitude, as observed in in Figure 5.6. The leads to a decrease in the ex-
pected risks, however, the benefits plotted in Figure 5.10 shows that the decision associated with the lowest
probability of system failure is associated with the lowest benefit, and so on. Therefore, the loss in revenue
caused by decrease of traffic, has a greater impact on the benefits than improvement of failure and damage

probabilities.
Table 5.14 tabulates calculated values for the life-cycle revenue, cost, risk and benefits of the different
actions linked to the strategy of no testing. The categorization of whether a decision is found acceptable or
not relates to the acceptance criteria assessment described in Section 5.2.3.
The highest revenue of 12,555 Mu is associated with the construction of a new bridge, consistent with
Figure 5.6 and Table 5.4. This indicates that the lowest system failure probability is found for the construction
of a new bridge, i.e. the highest probability of survival. The largest cost of 8467 Mu is related to construction
of a new bridge, where 5238 Mu, corresponding to approximately 62% of the 8467 Mu, are expenses related
to construction costs. The remaining 3229 Mu, or about 38%, are life time operation costs and tax expenses.

Based on the findings illustrated in Figure 5.10 and the listed values in Table 5.14, the decision with regard
to reclassifying the bridge can be evaluated. Since neither Class 200 or Class 175 were found acceptable
with regard to both the ultimate and serviceability limit state criteria, see Section 5.2.3.2 and 5.2.3.3, the
reclassification to Class 150 is now evaluated. A change in classification class, i.e. decrease of the traffic
load, results in lower cost and risk compared to the initial classification. The total expense includes both risk
and cost provides savings of 785 Mu, or about 21%, when reclassifying from Class 200 to Class 150, while
the loss of revenue is 2189 Mu ,or about 17%, which surpasses the expenses saved.
The life cycle benefit for Class 150 is 1404 Mu, or approximately 14%, lower than the life cycle benefit of
the initial Class 200 design. However, since only construction of a new bridge or reclassifying as Class 150
are acceptable solutions, in terms of the performance criteria, it is concluded that Class 150 reclassification is
found to be the optimal prior decision.
The cost benefit analysis is now expanded to include decisions related to additional information. The
analysis hereinafter no longer includes investigation of the decisions that does not fulfil the acceptance criteria,
see Section 5.2.3. Therefore, Class 200 with SHM is not taken into consideration and will be eliminated from
further investigation.
Buying additional information is related to some expense and/or risk related to the process of installing
and/or executing the test methods. Therefore, the following investigates the net income, which is the rev-

without accounting for any cost or risk related to purchase of a test method. This is done for the purpose of

Comparing the results of the strategy related to testing for Class 200 in Table 5.15 with the results in Table
5.14 of Class 200 without additional information, it is observed that the revenues are increased with 2 Mu,
or approximately 0.02%, and the costs are unchanged, when testing is chosen of Class 200. However, risks
are improved with a decrease of 398 Mu, about 67%, and 432 Mu, about 73%, for proof load and proof load
with SHM, respectively. Comparing Class 175 without additional information with Class 175 related to any
of the two test methods in Table 5.15, no significant changes with regard to the revenues and costs is observed.
The risks are decreasing 61 Mu, about 35%, and 38 Mu, about 21%, for SHM and proof load, respectively.
Therefore, the choice of test method does not have mentionable impact on neither the revenue or the cost, but
improvement of the risks related to the structural system is observed.
In Table 5.15 it is seen that the highest net income is associated to Class 200 related to proof load test with
SHM of Class 200 resulting in 9169 Mu, which is only 25 Mu, or about 0.3%, more than Class 200 linked
with proof load test. Therefore, the optimal decision amongst those two depends on whether the difference of
expenses associated with obtaining test methods is exceeding 25 Mu.

Figure 5.12 illustrates at year 1 that the lowest benefit is for the proof load test related to Class 200. Both
proof load test related to Class 200 and Class 175 show an increase at year 1. Since the net income did not
show a decrease at year 1, see Figure 5.11, it is not the system performance that results in this decrease, but
the expenses related to purchasing the proof load test methods. Also the proof load test with SHM linked to

to the risks caused by executing of a proof load test based on the knowledge that the cost of buying the test is
the same.

expected risks of load testing. For the test options involving SHM, it is expected to observe a small decrease
every decade due to installations and investments. However, the benefits does not reflect this tendency, mean-
ing that the net income exceeds the cost expenses.

Previously in this section it was found that the difference in net income of Class 200 related to proof load
test with SHM and proof load test were 25 Mu. The purchase of the proof load test is equal for both approaches
and without much further examination into the risks it might be assumed that proof load with SHM for Class
200 is not the optimal decision, since the installation and investment for SHM at year 1 is approximately 52
Mu which is already twice as much the difference of 25 Mu. However, Figure 5.12 indicates the opposite. As
observed in Table 5.17, the costs are 52 Mu and 201 Mu for the approach related to proof load and proof load
with SHM linked to Class 200, respectively. The cost of proof load with SHM is 149 Mu or about 300% more
expensive than the proof load test. However, the risks are 189 Mu, or about 27% lower than the proof load
test for Class 200. Therefore, the gained profit caused by lower risk of proof load with SHM for Class 200
exceeds the disadvantage associated with higher costs related to having both test system methods.
5.2.6.2
Risk assessment over service life
The risk assessment includes investigation of the direct and indirect risks. The risk indicators are further
quantified by the vulnerability and robustness indices, see Section 3.4. The direct risks are associated with the
direct consequences defined as those causing structural malfunction. The indirect risk is associated with the
indirect consequences which are follow-up consequences of the indirect consequences and results in structural
failure, see Sectiby estimated on 3.4.1. The risks are categorized as structural performance risks, RD,s and
RID,s, and the proof load test risks, RD,pl and RID,pl, 19.
Figure 5.13 presents the direct and indirect risks based on structural performance, this excludes test risks.
The direct risks encompasses the risks associated with component damage, component failure and system
damage. The highest direct consequence is associated with component failure, however, component damage
is related with the highest value of probabilities, which leads to component damage risk being the dominating
factor of the total direct risk RD,s, see Appendix B. Figure 5.13 shows that the proof load test of Class 200 is
computed to have the highest indirect risk, even though Figure 5.8 in Section 5.2.3.3 shows that the highest
probability of component damage at the end of service life is related to proof load with SHM for Class 200.
19Rs are the total risks based on structural performance, see Table 5.15. The total test risks related to proof load testing Rpl are
presented in Table5.16. The SHM system does not cause any risks on the structural system, see Section 5.2.5.

the probabilities of proof load of Class 200 which is impacting the results of the direct risk. Furthermore, the
component failure and system damage in Figure 5.7 and Figure 5.9 respectively, shows that the probabilities
related to proof load of Class 200 are greater than those related to proof load with SHM of Class 200, this also
influence the magnitude of direct risks.

over the years, which explains why proof load test with SHM for Class 200 and SHM for Class 175, is not
decreasing with the same steep slope at the other approaches after maximum robustness is reached. The total
risks are not increasing beyond the direct risks at same development rate.

5.2.6.3
Value of Information analysis
The Value of Information analysis (VoI) is based on the theory approach in Section 3.3.4. The pre-posterior
value of information analysis establishes the difference in benefit related to the optimal prior decision and
optimal pre-posterior decision for each of the test methods.
In Section 5.2.6.1 it was estimated that the optimal benefit related to proof load testing Bpl
1 is with regard to
Class 200. Reclassifying to Class 175 showed that the savings related to lower cost and risks, were surpassed
by the loss of revenue that also followed the decrease in load class. Therefore, this decision approach is
eliminated from further investigation.

Figure 5.12 presents the accumulated expected benefits of the optimal prior benefit, i.e. the strategy of no
testing, and the benefits of the action linked with additional information, that were found acceptable in relation
to the probability in the ultimate and serviceability limit state in Section 5.2.3. The Value of Information
analyses is established by the presented values.
In Figure 5.15 it is observed that all three test methods results in a negative value at year 1. The SHM
with load Class 175, shows a negative value at approximately −5 Mu at year 1, but it is positive again already
at year 2. Therefore, the expenses related to this approach are already more beneficial than the prior decision
two years after installation. However, test methods involving SHM also includes expenses related to a new
installation and investment at every decade. This payment is the reason why the VoI is lower at year 11 than
the previous year and every decade during the service life. The cost of the proof loading is a one time payment
at the year of the testing and the test method is related associated with risks. Therefore, the VoI is negative
the first 10 years for proof load test of Class 200. This means that this test method is not more beneficial
than the optimal prior benefit, before the VoI becomes positive. Unlike the SHM system which is related to a
continuous payment of operating and replacement every decade, the proof load only has the expenses at the
year of proof load testing. Therefore, the value of information is negative for a few years and the value of
information for Class 200 related to proof loading with SHM and does not increase the Class 175 related to
SHM before year 15. The proof load with SHM shows that even though the SHM is related to replacement
every decade, the revenue is high enough to still provide an increase of the value of information at every
decade. The results of the VoI at the end of service life is provided in Table 5.19.

5.2.6.4
Discussion of decision analysis
The following section discusses the VoI analysis, the cost-benefit analysis and the risk assessment with regard
to the optimal decisions for each individuel test methods and the strategy of no testing. Furthermore, the
implications and content of the knowledge related to the structural system results presented in the previous
sections are discussed.
114
==PAGE:146==
5.2 Descriptive illustration of reliability based capacity enhancement
In the previous sections, it was established that the optimal prior decision is associated with reclassifying to
Class 150, since neither a Class 200 or Class 175 were found sufficient for the acceptance criteria assessment.
A reconstruction was found to have the most beneficial revenue and lowest risk, but the high costs due to
reconstruction resulted in the benefit approximately 46% lower than the benefit of reclassifying to Class 150.

Table 5.20 summarizes the VoI and relative VoI results along with the indices related to vulnerability
and robustness. The optimal decision is proof load test with SHM for Class 200 yielding a relative value of
information of 0.15, i.e. the benefits are improved with 15% compared to the optimal prior benefit.
The optimal decision is seen to be associated with the highest robustness and second highest vulnerability.
The high indices of robustness indicate that the indirect risks do not impose any significant danger in relation
to the total risk. The vulnerability indices indicate the relation between the direct risks and the monetary value
of the bridge, hence low vulnerability is preferable. However, the most beneficial decision is associated with
a high index of vulnerability, since the higher revenue outbalance the disadvantages of the high risks. In the
Sections 5.2.6.1, 5.2.6.2 and 5.2.6.3, it was discussed at several occasions how this improvement of survival
probabilities does not outweigh the disadvantages of the loss of revenue caused by reclassification. Therefore,
observations and results obtained from the decision analysis is used to investigate the impact reclassification
has on the expected revenue and cost.
The magnitude of the yearly revenue of Class 175 and 150 is estimated in Appendix E.1.1 to obtain a life
cycle benefit with neglected risks equal to the initial benefit. Reclassifying to Class 175 should result in a
revenue of 651.92 Mu, which means an 10.12% increase of the current revenue of 592. Class 150 allows for a
revenue of 651.91, which corresponds to a 22.54% increase of the current 532 Mu. Including the risks results
in the revenues to be 626.19 Mu and 621.82 Mu, which means that survival probability increase only results
in a decrease of 0.0123% and 0.0135%, while also accounting for the risks results in a 3.96% and 4.63%. This
proves the state observations that the reclassification is not so much the action, but the effect on the risks that
improves the results of the life-cycle benefits, see Appendix E.1.25
This analysis outlines how the reported results and associated data can be used in a broader perspective to
asses different approaches. Knowledge obtained from the previous sections provides quantitative results that
can be used for determining whether some concerns might be worthwhile to investigate in depth before the
process of recalculating.
25The obtained values are approximations related to the strategy of no testing, where the risks are neglected in order to observe only
the impact of reclassification with regard to the revenue and costs.
115
==PAGE:147==
5 Applied Decision Analysis using SIS for Bridge Assessment
The following list presents examples where this informational data is used for fruitful discussions of the
bridge system from a management perspective, including predictions of the impact of different options and
their relevance to the overall system:
1. Tax rate increase.
To further elaborate on the practical usefulness of the calculation framework, a possible tax rate increase
can be used as an example. Such a tax rate increase could change the optimal decision to be a different
action since higher expected revenue is associated with higher tax expenses. A critical value for the
tax rate can be estimated, where the benefits related to proof load with SHM of Class 200 become less
than the benefits related to the prior option of reclassifying to Class 150, i.e. the VoI becomes negative.
The difference in benefits with no regard to the tax expenses is then 1433 Mu, which is approximately
65.11% of the difference in revenue. If the tax rate is increased above 65%, the VoI is calculated to be
negative. 26 The estimate of 65% is a 456% increase of the current tax rate of 14.28%. Therefore, it is
very unlikely that this scenario will become relevant at any time during the service life. See Appendix
E.1.1.
2. Required revenue increase to obtain initial benefit.
From an infrastructure perspective, any bridge is part of a larger road network and reclassification of
bridge structure results in re-routing of the trucks that are no longer allowed to pass the bridge. Often
the use of bridges shortens the travel distance, which leads to the possibility of increasing the cost price
without loosing any vehicles for situations where a price increase is preferred over re-routing. In this
case, the optimal decision should lead to a 3.35% increase in benefit compared to the initial benefit. It
is estimated in Appendix E.1.1 that a 2.64% increase, so about 669.18 Mu, of the revenue is required if
the life cycle benefit related to the optimal decision should increase to be equal to the initial benefit.
3. Infrastructure change.
Another scenario where the calculation framework is useful could be a change on infrastructure leading
to fewer trucks in load Class 200 passing the bridge. It is estimated in Appendix E.1.1 that a decrease
of 3.26% results in a revenue of 630.71 Mu. This would lead to that the optimal decision might change
to Class 175 with SHM. This decision is related to the lowest total expenses as seen in Table 5.20, but
the decrease in revenue when reclassifying resulted in decision options related to Class 200 being more
beneficial.
4. Increase of SHM installation and investment cost.
The optimal decision is associated with both proof load testing and SHM. Even though the SHM system
is associated with yearly operation costs and expenses related to installation and investment every 10th
year, the revenues related to the income are larger. The proof load test of Class 200 has a relative VoI
that is 0.89% lower. In Appendix E.1.1 it is estimated that if the SHM investment and installation cost is
26This is an approximation since the yearly probability is impacting the results and the changes.
116
==PAGE:148==
5.3 Chapter conclusion on applied decision analysis using SIS for bridge assesment
increasing above 50%, it might be worth to recalculate the decision analysis to determine if proof load
without SHM is more beneficial.
These examples provide insights into further usages of the reported values beyond the scope of this thesis.
The examples show that the computation of the case study results does not only provide information with
regard to the optimal test option, but it can also be used in many different forecasting situations from different
perspectives. Therefore, the investment associated with performing structural reliability and decision analysis
can further help decisions processes and aid the prediction of the consequences.
5.3
Chapter conclusion on applied decision analysis using
SIS for bridge assesment
In this chapter, a case study on decision analysis of structural information based on Class 200 Bridge was
presented and discussed. The theoretical framework developed in the previous chapters was used to asses the
optimal decision given a number of sources and classification of the bridge in terms of capacity. Specifically,
an approach for the quantification of structural health information sources and reclassification by load class
was utilized by the integrating Value of Information theory.
Firstly, a generic structural model for a perfect Daniels brittle system was presented in Section 5.1.1. Sec-
ondly, for the time-dependent structural system, a framework for a subsequently pre-posterior decision anal-
ysis was established and provided in Section 5.1.2. Thirdly, the specific modelling equations for the SHM
and proof load testing were presented and discussed from different perspectives in Section 5.1.3. Section 5.2
presented a descriptive illustration which incorporated the equations and assumptions for a generic model pre-
sented in Section 5.1 to 5.2. The decision framework was presented in a decision tree, this decision tool was
found to provide a clear overview of the branches of outcomes and actions related to the different strategies.
Section 5.2.2 presented the probabilistic characteristics and assumptions, together with the structural system
illustrated in a reliability network.
The initial system model was classified as a Class 200 bridge. In the design phase, the resistance and critical
damage value were calibrated with target reliabilities of 4.7 and 2.6, respectively. The initial probabilities were
presented in Section 5.2.3, where the initial design of the bridge was found to be insufficient at year 62, with
the target rehabilitates for a one year reference period of 3.7 and 1.3.
It was then decided to take initiative at year 50 in order to provide a reasonable probability margin to the
insufficient limit at year 62 and to provide an assessment of the bridge half way through its service life. For
the initiative taking at the beginning of year 51, two different strategies, testing or no testing, were presented.
The strategy of testing led to three options for test methods each considering adaptive actions linked with the
information acquirement strategy and the random outcomes of the information.
The associated revenue and cost expenses related to the bridge system and the tests were established from
data obtained from the Great Belt bridge and published research studies. The service life integrity management
117
==PAGE:149==
5 Applied Decision Analysis using SIS for Bridge Assessment
and risk models were presented for the purpose of carrying out a decision analysis. Section 5.2.6.1 presented a
cost benefit analysis. Even though the lowest failure and damage probabilities were related to reconstruction,
the optimal prior decision was related to reclassifying to Class 150, which meant a 14% loss compared to the
initial Class 200.
For the investigation of the net income it was seen that the strategy of testing provided an improvement
of the risks compared to prior results for similar classification classes. The risk assessment showed that the
direct risks has the dominating impact on the total risks and strategies of testing lead to decrease of the risks.
The expected life cycle benefits included the risks and cost associated with the purchase and executing
of the test methods. The results still showed that the loss of revenue caused by reclassification was greater
than the gained benefit. Therefore, the dominating influence with regard to the optimal decision is not the
improvement of the probabilities which effects the lower risks and higher survival, but related to the action
that the initial class can be kept, i.e. uphold the yearly earning as discussed in Section 5.2.6.2.
The optimal decision with regard to additional information was found to be performing proof load test with
SHM and not perform load class changes, i.e. the bridge remaining classified at Class 200. The benefit result-
ing in the highest relative VoI of 0.15 and the highest robustness of 0.97 and the second highest vulnerability
of 1.49 × 10−1.
In conclusion, it is clear that test methods improve the probabilities of the system. In general, obtaining
additional information is beneficial with respect to both the economic performance and the limit states over
the bridge lifetime. By implementing structural information sources, requirements can be met while still
maintaining the bridge without changing the load. Therefore, proof load testing at Class 200 is found to be
the most beneficial approach. Classification in terms of structural reliability within decision analysis may
be beneficial. The approach outlined in this thesis may benefit service life integrity management of critical
infrastructure.
The optimal test methods relates to the test methods of both proof loading and SHM for this specific case
study. However, it does not mean that multiple tests in all cases is the optimal decision. The decision maker
should take into account that the impact of test methods is different regarding which system performance state
is investigated. Structures like bridges in consequence class 3 (CC3), requires low probability of failure. SHM
does not contain any possibility of harming the structure, but it is not obtaining such new information. A proof
load test provides better information regarding the resistance and should be chosen in scenarios where there
is lack of knowledge with regard to the capacity.
118
==PAGE:150==
CHAPTER6
Conclusion
This thesis aimed at analysing efficient capacity enhancement of bridges using multiple structural information
sources, considering structural reliability. The study included reliability assessment of a simple bridge struc-
ture with the scope of a systematic and complete network reclassification of bridges. Chapter 1- 2 presented
the definition of reliability and concept from an engineering perspective, including literature survey with re-
gard to probability-based assessment of bridges including the presentation of the classification approach. The
capacity enhancement in the analysis was obtained by defining the class as the highest vehicle load it could
sustain and still satisfy the acceptance criteria. The acquired case study in Chapter 5 is utilized by the theoreti-
cal framework presented in Chapter 3.1. The mathematical formulation of structural reliability was presented
encompassing the Monte Carlo simulation technique, time-dependency of deterioration systems. Here after,
the approach proposed by DRD for classification of bridges by vehicle load class, encompassing target relia-
bilities at different limit states. This approach was adapted and used throughout the thesis work. The chapter
also presented Bayesian decision analysis including the VoI analysis in order to establish the optimal decision,
followed by risk assessment formulation, which quantified the vulnerability and robustness. Final, the concept
of structural information sources was presented encompassing SHM and proof load test. Furthermore, Chap-
ter 4 presented structural system illustrations for bridges in a reliability network. The illustrated work stress
the importance of a proper structural system illustration in order to obtain an appropriate reliability function.
The framework is applied to address the basic decision of implementing additional information or not for
a correlated structural formulation utilizing a Daniels brittle system. The reliability-based performance assess-
ment methodology accounts for time dependency by subsequently analysis. The decision analysis includes
the influence of lower structural capacity by loss of income hence the quantified life-cycle benefits account
for the expected income related to classification of the structural system.
The initial classification was found insufficient with regard to the acceptance criteria. It was decided to
take initiative halfway through the structure’s service life time at year 50. The optimal prior decision i.e. the
strategy of no testing was related to reclassification of Class 150, even though the action of reconstructing
resulted in lowest risks and highest income. However, the reconstruction costs surpassed the economical
advantage of higher capacity. The reclassification resulted in a decrease in life-cycle benefit of 14% compared
to the initial Class 200. The initial benefit was found to be 8745 Mu and the optimal prior decision had the
value of 7341 Mu.
The decision making framework encompasses the quantification of capacity classification linked with
structural health information sources integrating value of information analysis. The proposed structural sys-
tem modelling accounts for bridge performance under load effect and continues resistance reduction due to
deterioration.
119
==PAGE:151==
6 Conclusion
Three test options are quantified, SHM, proof load and proof load testing with SHM. The SHM system
monitors the load and the proof load test evaluates the resistance capacity of the structure. The quantification
facilitates the optimization of strategy by the optimization of the structural system risk and integrity manage-
ment.
The optimal decision with regard to additional information was found to be performing proof load test
with SHM for Class 200, with the lide-cycle benefit 8461. The VoI is 1121 Mu with the relative VoI of 0.15.
The index of vulnerability is 1.30 × 10−2 and index robustness is 0.97. The optimal decision is related to
higher risks than other decision strategies, it was concluded that the enhancement of the capacity is the main
reasoning for the quantified optimal value of information. The dominating influence with regard to the optimal
decision is not the improvement of the probabilities leading to lower risks and higher survival probability, but
the choice of action.
Although some of the presented advanced assessment methods require investment at the time of their
implementation that results in negative value of information, it has been shown that the additional information
improves the structural performance and result in an improvement of the total life-cycle benefit.
In conclusion, it is clear that structural information sources improve the capacity and uncertainty of the
probabilistic model. In general, obtaining additional information is beneficial with respect to both the eco-
nomic performance and the capacity over the bridge’s lifetime. By implementing structural information
sources, requirements of reliability targets can be met, while capacity enhancement allows for higher load
effect. The approach outlined in this thesis may benefit service life integrity management of critical infras-
tructure.
6.1
Outlook
• The quantified values is related to initiative taking at year 50 however, investigation utilizing the optimal
time, quantified by optimal test options should be considered. Furthermore, the case study only consid-
ered the option of choosing one test method and classification at year 50. Integration of optimal test
method and classification for each year over the service life, incorporating the performance assessment
related to quantified optimal option in previous years, should be considered.
• Various and new decision parameters, such as the number of proof load tested components, could be
examined in greater detail. The proof load test is assuming testing of all system components and it would
be interesting to quantify the optimal number of investigated components. Furthermore, the number of
proof load tests could be investigated, it might be more beneficial with regard to test risks and gaining
knowledge regarding the capacity could lower the structural system risk.
• The performance assessment methodologies presented in this case study should be integrated with the
other tasks of the life-cycle management framework such as optimization of inspection and repair activ-
ities and updating the performance with information from inspection and repairment.
120
==PAGE:152==
6.1 Outlook
• The theoretical approach for reliability networks was developed in Chapter 4 which enables the inclusion
of passive redundancy and is more comparable to architectural drawings than a block diagram. The
establishment of a correct reliability function is essential for the structural performance assessment, a
program utilizing structural reliability and the drawings of the structural design would be useful for
bridge design.
• Implementation of increasing traffic load over the service load, could also further be investigated in
order to provide forecasting models suitable for the changes in infrastructure.

probabilities are computed by the equations presented in Section 5.1.1 to 5.1.3 and the probabilistic character-
istics of the structural performance models which are presented in Section 5.2.2.

A.1.1.2

A.1.4
Prior probabilities - Class 150
The prior probabilities of failure and damage of Class 150 from year 50 to the end service life tsl = 100 years,
is presented for the system and all the components. The prior probabilities of Class 150 is associated with the
strategy of no testing.

A.2
Pre-posterior probabilities for SHM
The following presents the probabilities related to the pre-posterior decision analysis for SHM, i.e the strategy
associated with testing for the additional information obtained by SHM. The failure and damage probabilities
for both the structural system and the components are presented for classification Class 200 and Class 175.

years, is presented for the system and all the components. The pre-posterior probabilities of Class 200 is
associated with the strategy of testing related to SHM.

A.3
Pre-posterior probabilities for proof load test
The following presents the probabilities related to the pre-posterior decision analysis for proof load test, i.e the
strategy associated with testing for the additional information obtained by proof load test. The testing at the
year of test execution is associated with identification of the proof load test failure and damage probabilities
which are presented in the following. The probabilities related to structural performance are presented for the
failure and damage probabilities for both the structural system and the components for classification Class
200 and Class 175.
A.3.1
Probabilities for proof load test - Class 200
The testing process with the test load 375 for Class 200 is associated with identification of the proof load test
failure and damage probabilities at year of testing tp = 1, which is presented. Furthermore, the probabilities
of failure and damage for proof load test for Class 200 from year 50 to the end of service life, tsl = 100 years,
are presented for the system and the components. The pre-posterior probabilities of Class 200 is associated
with the strategy of testing related to proof load testing.
A.3.1.1

f,s
and the compo-
nents P Ipl,200
f,i
, where i = 1..n and n being the number of components which is equal to 5, for the bridge
structure classified as Class 200 associated with the strategy of testing related to proof load testing.

A.3.2
Probabilities for proof load test - Class 175
The testing process with the test load 317 for Class 175 is associated with identification of the proof load test
failure and damage probabilities at year of testing tp = 1, which is presented. Furthermore, the probabilities
of failure and damage for proof load test for Class 175 from year 50 to the end service life, tsl = 100 years, are
presented for the system and all the components. The pre-posterior probabilities of Class 175 are associated
with the strategy of testing related to proof load testing.

A.4
Pre-posterior probabilities for proof load test with SHM
The testing process with the test load 363 with SHM for Class 200 is associated with identification of the proof
load test failure and damage probabilities at year of testing tp = 1, which are presented. Furthermore, the
probabilities of failure and damage for proof load test with SHM for Class 200 from year 50 to the end service
life, tsl = 100 years, are presented for the system and all components. The pre-posterior probabilities of Class
200 are associated with the strategy of testing related to proof load testing with SHM.
157

Probabilities for proof load test with SHM - Class 200

tsl = 100 years, are presented for the system and all the components. The pre-posterior probabilities of Class
200 are associated with the strategy of testing related to proof load testing with SHM.

related to the structural system performance and the costs of operation, installation and investment related to

and total risks presented together with the net income and the total costs related to the SHM system including
the benefit results for the pre-posterior analysis for the strategy of testing related to SHM.

B Cost-Benefit Analysis
B.3
Pre-posterior benefits of proof load test
Following presents the results of the annual expected revenue, operation cost and tax expenses and the risks
related to the structural system performance and the risks and investment cost with regard to the proof load
test. Furthermore, is the cumulative expected revenue, total costs related to the structural system and total
risks presented together with the net income and the total cost and risk related to the proof load test including
the benefit results for the pre-posterior analysis for the strategy of testing related to proof load testing.
B.3.1

following. Table B.14 presents the annual probabilities of the expected revenues, tax expenses, cost operation
and the risks associated with component damage, risk of component failure and the total of the three risks
results in the direct risk which is presented along with the indirect risk.

the benefit results for the pre-posterior analysis for the strategy of testing related to proof load testing.

APPENDIXC
Risk Assessment
The following consists of the computed results of the risk assessment. The concept of direct and indirect
risks along with the vulnerability and robustness indices are presented in Chapter 3.4 and the framework is
presented in Section 5.2.5. The probabilities required for the risk assessment is presented in Appendix A.
C.1
Direct and indirect consequences
Equation (5.33) presents the direct risk related to the environmental exposures, i.e. excluding exposure of
the proof load test. The direct risk consists of the risk associated with system damage presented in Equation
(5.31), risk related to component failure in Equation (5.30) and component damage in Equation (5.29). The
probabilities are presented in Appendix A and are Ps,d, Pf,i and Pd,i, where i = 1..n, n being the number of
components, which for this specific case study is n = 5. The associated direct consequences are presented
in Table B.1, the direct consequences related to system damage cD (d, s) = Cd,s, the direct consequences
cD (f, i) are the component failure cost Cf,i and the direct consequences related to component damage is
cD (d, i) = Cf,i.
The indirect risk is presented in Equation (5.34), which is the risk associated with system failure, see
Equation (5.32). The system failure probability Pf,s is presented in Appendix A, the direct consequence is
related to the in-service failure cost Cf,in and the loss of income from the year of failure to end of service
life. The indirect consequences can be separated into two indirect consequences, direct consequence of the
cost of in-service failure cID (f, s) which is equal to Cf,in, which is presented in Table B.1, and indirect
consequence associated with the loss of income related system failure at the time of failure to end of service
life cID (I). The income is established as the revenue minus the operation and tax expenses, all three variables
are presented in Table B.2. These are used in the establishment of the indirect consequences related to the
income cID (I), which are depended on the classification class and presented in Table C.1 together with the
total indirect consequences.
Table C.1: The indirect consequences related to income loss from year of failure to end of service life together
with total direct consequences CID for each classification class, all values are presented in Mu.
td [Year]
